World's First Trillionaire, Anthropic Fable Banned, The New Oligarchs, Iran Peace Deal

model and the...

TL;DR

The All-In Podcast analyzes the high-stakes intersection of technology and state intervention, highlighting SpaceX’s historic $85 billion public offering and $60 billion Cursor acquisition alongside the federal shutdown of Anthropic’s "Fable 5" AI model and the geopolitical implications of the US-Iran peace memorandum.

The Rising "Politburo" and the Erosion of Human Agency

  • The Great American Politburo: David Friedberg warns that a self-electing class of political leaders (naming Elizabeth Warren, Bernie Sanders, and Ro Khanna) is attempting to dictate the flow of capital, education, and media.
    • Erosion of Private Property: Pointing to Illinois's new asset and cryptocurrency tax laws, Friedberg cautions that allowing governments to tax assets after income taxes are paid marks a dangerous shift away from foundational American property rights.
    • Virtue-Signaling as Control: The promise of "free" state benefits (childcare, food, healthcare) is framed as a trap that reduces individual economic mobility and makes citizens wards of the state.
  • The Psychology of Learned Helplessness:
    • Chamath Palihapitiya's Welfare Experience: Chamath shares his personal background growing up on welfare in Canada (living on $17,000–$19,000 annually for a family of five). He notes that even a low threshold of state support can inadvertently sap personal drive and trap families in cycles of dependency and depression.
    • The Problem-Solving Spectrum: If individuals are presented with impossible problems first, they succumb to learned helplessness; if they are given accessible, progressively harder problems, they build agency and career momentum.

SpaceX’s Historic IPO and the $60B Cursor Acquisition

  • A Record-Breaking Offering: SpaceX went public at $135 per share, raising $85 billion—three times the capital raised by Saudi Aramco in 2019. The stock surged to $177 at taping, briefly positioning SpaceX as the fourth most valuable company globally before settling at seventh.
  • The Cursor Acquisition: SpaceX executed its option to acquire AI coding assistant Cursor for $60 billion, representing a 15x multiple on Cursor’s $4 billion revenue run-rate.
    • Strategic Stock Swap: Chamath notes that because Elon Musk paid in "tomorrow's dollars" using rapidly appreciating SpaceX equity, Musk effectively secured Cursor at a massive discount (the equivalent of roughly $15 billion in real terms).
  • The Nature of Wealth: David Sacks addresses the public backlash to Musk becoming the world's first "trillionaire," clarifying that paper wealth is not liquid cash but the discounted present value of the machines that make physical goods.
    • Labor to Capital Transition: Historically, the tech industry allows workers to transition from labor to capital ownership via equity. Sacks shares the story of a SpaceX welder who made $1 million in stock, demonstrating the fluid, non-Marxist nature of modern capitalism.
    • Democratization of IPOs: Jason Calacanis highlights that 20% to 30% of the SpaceX IPO was allocated directly to retail users on Robinhood and Charles Schwab, allowing roughly 600,000 to 700,000 everyday investors to participate.

The Federal Intervention of Anthropic’s "Fable 5" Model

  • The "Mythos" Cyberweapon: Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei previously pitched the "Mythos" model to Washington officials as an incredibly powerful tool with advanced cyber capabilities, raising security anxieties.
  • The Security Leak: Anthropic expanded its "trusted partners" preview program to over 50 entities without notifying the White House. This included South Korea's SK Telecom, which national security officials flagged due to suspected backchannel links to China.
  • The Amazon Whistleblower & Federal Ban:
    • The Guardrail Failure: Amazon (AWS), a key Anthropic shareholder and partner, stress-tested "Fable 5" (the public, guard-railed version of Mythos) and discovered a severe jailbreak vulnerability. AWS escalated the security flaw to the White House.
    • The Standoff: Commerce Secretary Howard Lutnick called Amodei to demand the model be restricted. Amodei reportedly downplayed the risk, arguing pedantically in a blog post about "minor vs. major" jailbreaks and refusing to pull it down immediately.
    • The Export Control: The White House issued an export control letter, forcing Anthropic to pull the model globally while they renegotiated security protocols.
  • The Threat of Hyperscaler Gatekeeping: Chamath warns that frontier labs’ failure to cooperate with state security demands creates a massive business opportunity for hyperscalers (Amazon, Microsoft, Google) to pitch themselves as exclusive "adult supervision" gatekeepers, wrapping AI in strict Know Your Customer (KYC) requirements.

Claude's Psychoanalysis of Creator Dario Amodei

  • Epistemic Exceptionalism: Chamath prompted Anthropic's own Claude model to analyze Dario Amodei’s essays ("Machines of Loving Grace"). Claude generated a psychological profile diagnosing Amodei with "epistemic exceptionalism."
    • The "Me" Machine: Claude wrote that because Amodei distrusts governments, competing labs, and markets, his safety framework inevitably concludes that only his own reasoning is pure enough to hold the keys to AGI.
  • Tribal Alignments in AI: Sacks argues that Anthropic is deeply aligned with the Democratic establishment, pointing out that multiple top AI policy figures from the Biden administration immediately transitioned to roles at Anthropic. Conversely, companies like OpenAI and xAI have key figures aligned with the Trump camp.

Trump Codifies the Iran War Peace MOU

  • The Memorandum of Understanding (MOU): After 110 days of war, President Trump announced an MOU to end the conflict. Mediated by Pakistan, the deal includes a 60-day ceasefire, a Lebanese ceasefire, and the reopening of the vital Strait of Hormuz.
  • Nuclear Disarmament: Iran committed to freezing its nuclear enrichment program and destroying its current stockpile of enriched uranium under IAEA supervision. Friedberg notes that physically removing enriched uranium from the country effectively resets Iran's weaponization timeline by 10 to 15 years.
  • Financial and Reconstruction Terms: While Iran will receive sanctions relief and access to frozen assets, a $300 billion reconstruction fund will be financed by Gulf states, leaving US taxpayers entirely unburdened.
  • Debating the Alternatives: Sacks defends the MOU against neoconservative critics demanding regime change, pointing out that invading Iran—a mountainous country three times the size of Iraq—would require over a million US troops and result in a catastrophic, endless war.

Notable Quotes

"Economic mobility is the key to the United States. You do not get economic mobility if the government is giving you a job or giving you money. You lose economic mobility. You become indentured to the government." — David Friedberg
"There’s a big difference between stuff and then the machines that make the stuff... The way that people get wealthy is that if you create a machine that makes more stuff, then there’s a discounted present value for all the stuff in the future that that machine might create." — David Sacks
"When your safety framework requires that someone hold the keys, and your analysis keeps concluding the other key-holders can't be trusted, you've built a machine that outputs 'me' no matter what you feed it." — Claude (Anthropic AI, analyzing Dario Amodei)
"There is a deep arrogance to technologists... We all assume that this time is different and everything's about to change completely. And you know what? It fucking doesn't. We're all part of this long-term continuum of technological and productivity improvements driven by human ingenuity." — David Friedberg

And the super sinister part, to dovetail what both of you are saying and to give some grace to your dad, Chamath, in the studies of learned helplessness... For people who are trying to guess what that term means who haven't heard it before, it's a psychological condition. If you give people a hard problem to solve, like an impossible problem to solve, they don't solve it. Then you give them easy problems to solve, they don't solve the easy ones, and they become depressed. And then they attribute their depression to themselves. They internalize it, in other words. If you give people easy problems to solve and make them increasingly harder as you go, they become empowered. They have agency. They feel like they can solve problems. And then when they get stuck on something, they don't attribute it internally. And this is why alcoholism is so pernicious in our society as well, because when you start attributing it to yourself and then you start self-medicating, you try to dull these feelings because you’re depressed. And I think this is very important for people to understand: there’s a big difference between stuff and then the machines that make the stuff. And I think, again, this is where wealth comes from—it is the machines that make the stuff. So you know, you go all the way back to hunter-gatherer days, right? The only wealth was in what you could collect, hunt, gather, whatever. It was basically the collection of stuff. Then humans developed the ability to create tools, and then very sophisticated tools. And then some of those tools are corporations, which are almost like cybernetic organisms—or combinations of tools, workflows, humans work in them. And it’s those corporations that make the stuff. And the way that people get wealthy is that if you create a machine that makes more stuff, then there’s a discounted present value for all the stuff in the future that that machine might create. And that’s where the wealth comes from. Really well said. It’s not in the stuff; it’s in creating a machine that will create stuff for humanity for a long time, and people will put a value on it today because of all the stuff that it’ll create in the future. And so just out of nowhere, it will appear that all of a sudden you have all this wealth created. But again, that wealth is a reflection of all the stuff that this machine will now create in the future. And people need to understand that that process is a good thing because when you think about all the progress that humanity has made, it’s not in having more stuff. Because again, that stuff all goes away, right? Whether it’s food or shelter or clothes. It’s ephemeral. It depreciates. It’s a wasting asset. The reason why humans are more prosperous is because of all the machinery we've created to make stuff well into the future. And you know, again, some of that stuff can be things like shelter, clothes; it can also be medicines and cures and entertainment, electricity—all the things that make your life better. So we need those machines. And someone has got to own the machines. And the people who own them are the ones who create it. And the thing that Karl Marx never understood is that it’s not this sharp delineation between capital and labor, because you can take people who start with nothing. Elon started with nothing. I mean, he was an immigrant to the United States. He came here from nothing. Slept on the floor at Zip2. Slept on the floor. He created these companies, he created these machines from nothing with his own vision and hard work. And he included thousands of other people. So here’s the other thing: just because you're quote unquote labor doesn't mean that you’re not also an owner of capital. There’s a great story about how there’s a welder who worked at SpaceX who I think has made a million dollars in SpaceX stock. Transformational. But everybody who’s part of these companies—and I think this is the magic of the tech industry—it is much more inclusive in terms of ownership than any other industry. It’s the magic of capitalism in free markets where anyone can participate in that market, anyone can enter that market, and ultimately anyone can transition from being labor to capital. Yes. It’s fluid. It’s back and forth. If you buy shares in a company and you have the ability to buy shares. Or you just vest them. Or you vest them with sweat equity. Or you start a business and you basically do work, save money, invest your capital, and continue to compound the capital until you get to a point that your labor is worth less than your capital. And that’s what ultimately allows this economic mobility, this transition for people. And when you take that away, you destroy that capacity for mobility. That’s why it’s capitalism where we started today, yeah. That’s right. And that’s why we started today with agency. That’s why it’s so frustrating to me to watch people denounce Elon’s success and denounce the value that the world is putting on this business and on this success. Because what they’re saying is that no future human, no kid that’s sitting on a floor right now, sleeping on a floor that’s poor, should have the capacity to achieve what Elon achieved. No one should be allowed that capacity to transition, to progress, to mobilize themselves. It’s even worse than that, Friedberg. They’re saying you should hate him. By denouncing him, and by denouncing that, you are basically taking that ability away. And you’re saying that this is evil. Can I take a shot at answering Chamath’s question: why are there so many people who are influential who are resentful of what Elon’s done or what other capitalists do? I think it was Joseph Schumpeter who explained this. That basically you’ve got this intelligentsia in a society like ours where they don't make things, they don't make the machines that make things. They just make words and, you know, I guess ideas. And most of those ideas are just wrong, right? They’re just made up. And they become resentful of the fact that there are all these people who are able to create, not just the stuff, but the machines that make the stuff, right? And that in our society, that is the thing that creates the greatest reward financially. Why? Because it actually aligns with what’s in the greatest value for society. Whereas they produce a bunch of hot air. So it’s natural for that class of intelligentsia to become resentful and hostile towards the people who are able to amass this great wealth. I think that the great lie is that there are two sides to the society, that is the rich and the poor. And the great truth is that there are two sides that are the makers and the takers. The lie is that the rich are unfairly rich and the poor are unfairly poor. And therefore the poor must take from the rich. But the truth is that it’s the takers that tell you that lie. That the real truth is that artists, plumbers, electricians, woodworkers, computer scientists, people that build, people that make shit from all walks of life, all income levels, all wealth brackets are the makers. And the takers are what Sacks calls this intelligentsia. The analysts. The espousers. The armchair mechanics. The critics. The commentators. The politicians. They are the takers. They are the people that watch the rest of society make stuff, build stuff, specifically doing things that create value for other people in society. That’s what a maker is. I tell my kids this lesson all the time. I say, "What did you guys make today?" They tell me something they made. And I'm like, "Did someone else value it? Or did you make it just for yourself?" At the end of the day, if you made something and someone else valued it, you were a maker. That was an amazing achievement. That is a great day. Whether you make a piece of art, whether you build a house, whether you write a piece of software, whether you build a business. Whatever you make, if you’re a maker and someone else values it, they become your customer in some way, they become your partner in some way, you've done something valuable. Those are the true engines of progress for humanity. And the takers are the ones that tell the lie that it’s rich versus poor. Because what they want to do is rip apart the makers and they want to tell everyone you got to be on one side or the other, and they use that lie to get everyone to line up against each other and to give themselves ultimately the control to form the great American Politburo. That's why I think that there's currently a natural market force for two things: one is more open source, and second is a fragmentation of the AI stack. If you go back to the 1960s, IBM was the predominant provider of mainframe computers. And I don't know if you guys remember this or know this, but like, at the time, IBM did the whole stack. They actually made the chips. All of the, you know, I think it was solid logic technology chips that IBM created were made in-house. They made the hardware. They made the operating system. They also made the software. And this was the case through the whole mainframe technology era, that IBM was the monopoly—they had the whole thing. And then ultimately the government had to intervene to get them to disaggregate the software layer. And that's what actually started the independent software vendor era, which is when the software industry really started, was after the government said, "Hey, we shouldn't have one company that has the whole stack in computing." Then we get to the '80s, and that's when everything disaggregated. Then IBM started buying the Intel chip for the personal computer. They had their own OS, but ultimately Microsoft came along and made a better OS. And then this entire proliferation of the software stack with independent vendors took off. And I think that that ultimately is what allowed this industry to progress. The productivity boom that arose from both mainframe computing and desktop PCs was because of this disaggregation that happened. I think the same is likely going to have to happen with these AI companies. And, you know, I'm not sure that what we're looking at today as being kind of some oligopoly of three or four businesses is really where we're going to stand. I think the equivalent on the hardware side is there's going to be multiple chip vendors. There's also going to be multiple clouds. There's also going to be multiple models that run on the clouds. People are also going to run models locally. They're going to use different software factories. They're not just going to use Anthropic. They're going to use tools like Salesforce and, you know, 80/90 and other things. They're going to run different applications on top of it that are going to be purpose-built or custom-built. So I think there's very likely kind of a diffusion that's happening right now. If it doesn't happen, it's going to be pretty nasty. But I just think the market forces are so strong for a diffusion of each layer in the stack finding its way to the cheapest, the more available, the more accessible partner vendors that we're very likely going to see these kind of, you know, handful of oligopolies kind of break down a little bit. The more precise term is epistemic exceptionalism: not 'I am superior,' but 'my reasoning is the load-bearing one, and the failure of others to reach my conclusions is evidence of their corruption or slowness, not of my error.' That's the genuinely concerning pattern, and it's worth naming plainly because it's the engine of the dangerous version. Once you believe your judgment is the uncorruptible reference point, every conflict gets re-coded. Government overreach isn't 'powerful actors have competing interests and I lost'—it's 'they failed to be transparent and fair.' The Media Game Has Changed

a16z(278.0K)·41:52·4.6K views·2026-06-19
Marc AndreessenCo-founder, Andreessen Horowitz Ben HorowitzCo-founder, Andreessen Horowitz Erik TorenbergCo-founder, Turpentine
TL;DRThe media landscape has shifted from a defensive, centralized era of corporate branding to a decentralized "go-direct" era where individual authenticity is the primary competitive advantage.
To build a modern audience, founders must abandon sterile media-trained...

TL;DR

The media landscape has shifted from a defensive, centralized era of corporate branding to a decentralized "go-direct" era where individual authenticity is the primary competitive advantage. To build a modern audience, founders must abandon sterile media-trained personas, embrace being interesting (even at the cost of drawing critics), and frame their companies through "outside-in" global narratives rather than self-focused product updates.

## The Transition from Legacy Media to a "Go Direct" Strategy

  • The Fall of Legacy Media Relevance: Since 2017, traditional media has shifted from objective, explanatory journalism to an adversarial, activist posture.
      • No Winning Strategy: Marc Andreessen emphasizes that it is virtually impossible for a tech startup to secure a consistently fair or positive narrative through traditional press anymore. Individual hits are possible, but a sustainable legacy PR strategy is dead.
      • The Prestige Shift: Prestige has migrated to decentralized channels. Even in Washington, D.C., key decision-makers and senators now prioritize independent newsletters like Mark Halperin’s over legacy institutions like *The Washington Post*.
  • The "Go Direct" Mandate: Companies must bypass traditional gatekeepers and tell their stories through owned channels and allied networks.
  • Fights as Brand Accelerators: Ben Horowitz notes that responding strategically to major public attacks can dramatically elevate a brand.
      • The Instagram Kerfuffle: A public pushback by a16z against *The New York Times* regarding Instagram became their highest-performing content piece of that era because "everyone loves a fight."
      • The Engagement Rule: Ignore minor trolls (e.g., low-follower accounts on X), but aggressively seize high-profile adversarial moments to clarify your worldview.

## Authenticity and the "Brand as Person" Era

  • The Decline of "Plastic" Media Training: In the 1990s, corporate executives were trained to be as boring and innocuous as possible to minimize risk. Modern communication requires the exact opposite: speaking in public with the same unfiltered visceral energy you would use when having lunch with a friend.
  • The Person is the Brand:
      • Historical Context: In the 1930s and 40s, the rise of highly centralized media (three TV networks, limited print space) forced companies to distill their identities into abstract, corporate brands (e.g., General Motors, IBM, Tide).
      • The Decentralized Return: In the internet era, distribution channels are unlimited, and human connection is paramount. Audiences associate companies with distinct personalities rather than abstract logos: Elon Musk represents SpaceX, Alex Karp represents Palantir, and Palmer Luckey represents Anduril.
  • The "Joe Rogan" Bar: Modern leaders must be capable of sitting through three-hour, unscripted podcast discussions. If a founder cannot hold an audience's attention in this format, they face a severe marketing deficit.
  • The Danger of Neutrality: To be interesting, you must accept polarization. If nobody hates you, nobody loves you either. Attempting to please everyone results in uninteresting, sterile corporate messaging.

## Mastering "Outside-In" Narrative Strategy

  • The Inside-Out Trap: Most startups rely on self-absorbed narratives ("We raise money, we went on a walk, we launched a product"). These narratives are boring, indistinguishable, and fail to engage anyone outside the company's immediate bubble.
  • The Outside-In Hack: Position your startup within a massive, highly compelling global trend that is already captured in the public consciousness.
      • The Alex Karp Playbook: The Palantir CEO rarely explains what Palantir does in technical terms. Instead, he addresses global conflict, AI integration in the military, and geopolitics. Because he is the loudest voice on these critical topics, Palantir becomes the default solution in the minds of the public and policymakers.
      • The Ryan Petersen Playbook: Rather than pitching Flexport as a freight-forwarding company, Petersen went on *60 Minutes* during the COVID-19 pandemic to discuss global supply chain collapse and food security. The connection to Flexport's business remained implicit but incredibly powerful.
  • Enterprise Credibility: Enterprise customers and government officials do not want to meet with a generic vendor; they want to meet with leaders who understand the macroeconomic and geopolitical forces shaping the world.

## Tactics and Pitfalls for New Media Teams

  • Message Over Distribution: Gaby Goldberg points out that founders frequently over-index on distribution tactics (e.g., "How do we go viral?" or "How do we get on Joe Rogan?") before defining a clear message. Strong distribution on a weak message only amplifies irrelevance.
  • Work Backward From Outcomes: Start with your business objectives—such as recruiting top-tier engineers or closing enterprise sales—and design the message specifically for that target audience, rather than vomiting every corporate milestone into the public domain.
  • Hiring for the New Paradigm:
      • Avoid Legacy PR Mindsets: Traditional PR professionals who have spent decades vetting reporters and managing defensive Q&As find it incredibly difficult to operate in a direct-to-audience, offensive framework.
      • Hire Native Storytellers: Build marketing and communication teams around individuals who have a proven track record of creating compelling content, building organic audiences, and understanding the digital "discourse."

Notable Quotes

"The one rule of old media is don't be interesting. Like, that's the worst thing you can do... New media is unlimited formats on unlimited channels, and the brand is now the person." — Ben Horowitz
"The story of you and your startup is not inherently an interesting story, but there is almost certainly an interesting story that involves your startup. And that story is... something else in the world that's happening that is incredibly interesting, that your company relates to." — Marc Andreessen
"A lot of founders really over-index on distribution and tactics before actually getting the message right. Distribution is really just a multiplier on the message, and so if the message is wrong, now you've amplified something that is either irrelevant for your business... or, worst of all, it's uninteresting." — Gaby Goldberg
"You really want people to hate you and you want people to love you, but you don't want to be neutral. You don't want to be lukewarm... as soon as you get big that's what happens. And that's good. Because that means you did it. You did something important." — Marc Andreessen

Yeah, so the press in my view—and reporters get furious at me when I say this, which is why I know that I'm correct—the traditional press, legacy press defined itself as having two functions, right? And of course, at this point, we know what that means. Yeah. Well, it evolved into power to truth. So it's like the press's way of intimidating you into not saying what you thought. "Yeah, because there's a line between actually objective and activist, right? Somebody with an agenda." And so, my view is what happened over time is that that second one just swamped the first one, and it just became the thing. As a consequence, the very nature of the interaction changed. And I mean, the short version is I did tons of traditional media between 1994 and 2017. Every once in a while, there was a hit piece or a bad faith thing or whatever, but like, 90% of the time I felt like it had been a good idea to do it, and I felt like I had been given a chance to tell my story. By the way, for almost that entire run, most people in the country and in the press thought startups were kind of cool, and tech was kind of cool and fun, and it was kind of good for America that there were these tech companies and the products were kind of fun to use. "I kind of remember those days." Yeah, way back in the ancient myths of the past. You know, everything was exciting. A lot of reporters or editors genuinely viewed it as like, "Oh, we need to explain this to our readers and our viewers and really have this come across." And then, as we like to say, in 2017 things changed. Basically since then, my view is that that second mode of being—which is basically the agenda-driven kind of mode—really took over, which I think probably will never actually unwind. And so, it's just the practical reality, as we always tell our founders: there is no way to get to anything resembling a story that you're going to like through the traditional media anymore. It is basically not possible. "You can land a story, but you can't run a strategy, yeah." Yeah, you'll always find people who point to individual success, and I'll just be like, "Okay, that's the exception that proves the rule. There are 99 others that are not like that." And so, there just needs to be a new approach. I think the new approach comes in two parts. It comes in what we all call now "go direct," which is you have to tell your own story, and increasingly through your own channels and through the channels of allies. But there's still this anxiety that people have, which is legacy media somehow is where the respectability or the prestige is. I thought they all read this old stuff, and not even that is true. So, I think Marc's right. Marc, you mentioned going direct. We're certainly in the era now of going direct, and it feels like it's table stakes for founders. You can even think of a lot of great founders who spent their lives or careers in a lab or staring at a computer, and this is not what they've been trained in. So I'm curious about both of your advice for founders who maybe are uninterested in doing this or they don't know where to start. Do they have to get good at it, or are there other paths for them to get their company the attention it deserves? Yeah, so I think this is tricky. If you look at old media and how that worked versus new media: in old media, you had very restricted channels with very restricted formats, and the brands were the companies. That is basically the setup. Yes, you could talk to the New York Times or CNN or whatever if you wanted to get the word out, but then you were forced into a quote or a very short interview, and you were representing a brand that wasn't you. That's just kind of how it worked. And the whole media strategy basically was just to get your name out there without tainting the company to the point where it could never recover, which is kind of how you got into this very defensive posture. You can never take back anything; that was like a big rule in old media. Then you get to new media, and new media is the opposite in that it's unlimited formats, unlimited channels, and the brand is now the person. When every Democrat came out and was mad about SpaceX, they weren't; they were mad about Elon because he's a brand. And the same thing: is it Palantir, or is it Alex? Is it Anduril, or is it Palmer? That's just over and over again. The companies that are winning in marketing, the brand is the person. If you had another founder or someone who is a real permanent fixture and not the CEO, you could imagine that person becoming the brand, but it's going to be a person. I don't think there's a way around that. And the rule of new media is it has to be interesting, because otherwise it's going to get drowned out. You have to be a person, and that person has to be interesting, which is why old media is so dangerous. The one rule of old media is: don't be interesting. That's the worst thing you can do; you'll eff everything up. So, I don't think a company can get away without it. If you look at us as a firm, technically I'm the CEO, but Marc is more the brand, in terms of doing way more media than I do. And that works, because it's Andreessen Horowitz and nobody knows the difference on the outside—it's fine. You can set up your company that way, and that's fine. But it has to be somebody who is there kind of forever with the organization. It can't be the Vice President of Marketing who is here for a three-year run and then gone; that will never work. "Anduril is another example." Yeah, yeah. One of my favorite things that happens about once a week is when somebody congratulates me on writing Ben's book. "Yeah, which he always takes full credit for." I do, I do! It's just like, gosh, I really poured all my blood, sweat, and tears into that book. I'm so glad someone finally read it. So, look, I think there is actually a technological explanation for what Ben said that is quite important. Up until the 1930s or something, companies were not—they didn't have corporate brands. You had people. It was the Ford Motor Company, the Edison Electric Company. It was literally the name on the door. It never even occurred—I'm positive it never occurred to Henry Ford to name his company anything other than the Ford Motor Company. "Well, he did have a company before the Ford Motor Company, right?" Yeah, which was like—I can't remember, somebody's got to look it up. I think it became Cadillac or something. It was actually a car company that lasted, but he was CTO, not CEO, and he got in such a furious fight because he was a hothead, Henry Ford, with the CEO that he quit his own company. "Yeah, well, a lot of those are Chrysler—like Chrysler, they were those guys. A lot of the car companies..." Oh yeah, they are all named after themselves. I think actually maybe it was Ford, and when he quit they renamed it. That could be it. "Yeah, yeah, something else." So it was like name on the door, and it was just taken as a given because that's the guy running the company, and that's what you do. And then I think what happened is in the '30s and '40s, you started getting these abstract names—International Business Machines, General Electric... "General Motors." General Motors. You started getting these abstract names, and then you got the consumer brand thing with Procter & Gamble, so you had Tide and Colgate, Crest toothpaste, and Coca-Cola. "Yeah, the Nifty 50, the conglomerate era, yeah." Which led to the conglomerates, which were basically just piling up as many brands as you could. In retrospect, I think that was because of the rise of centralized media specifically. Before the 1930s, media was very decentralized—every town had like 15 newspapers, there were tiny little radio stations everywhere. And then, starting in the '30s or '40s, media centralized hard in a way that was historically unprecedented, probably a once-in-a-thousand-year thing. The thing with centralized media was that it's like drinking everything through an incredibly narrow straw. By definition, there are three TV networks and 24 hours in the day. How much time are you ever going to get? Or if there's the front page of three big newspapers, how many column inches are you ever going to get? The message from any company had to get distilled down to the absolute minimum to be able to get through those very narrow straws. I think that was the corporate brand phenomenon: to get the thing down to an atomic unit of a brand to get it through that straw. Everybody became convinced that was universally the way the world worked, but I think company as an abstract brand only made sense in the centralized media world. What we're seeing now, as the centralized media world is unwinding and collapsing, is mechanically why this new approach is working and is necessary. In the new world, do people running for president have to go on Joe Rogan? Up until 2024, the answer was absolutely not. After 2024, the answer is 100% they have to. Keeping politics out of it, a big part of all the Democratic retrospectives of what happened in '24 was that Kamala didn't go on Rogan. 100% of the people who work in that world now think that the next person is going to have to go on Rogan. To go on Rogan means they're going to have to be on Rogan for three hours, and you're going to have to talk about anything. If you think about what that means for the bar for the person who is going to run for any party, that's the new bar. You have to be able to do that. You have to be interesting, and then it goes right back to your question: you have to be the person who can do that. If you're not the person who can do that, you're at a big marketing deficit, let's just put it that way. You just put a ceiling on your whole opportunity, I think. The line from Succession, of course—I can't avoid using it based on your question—is what Shiv said: "If you can't ride two elephants at the same time, what are you doing in the circus?" I would love to hear how you apply this thinking to your own work. Yeah, so, I mean, it's interesting with that, because anytime somebody says something negative about us, we want to respond. "It'll always improve your situation to push back." Yeah, Marc always 100% wants to go back at them. You have to have some discipline, otherwise you will spend your whole life responding. The bigger you get, the more people come at you. It's kind of like the degenerate version of dealing with the people who comment on Twitter—or X, sorry. "Well, to that end, when it comes to picking battles, when I first joined I would bring up these negative tweets we were getting and say, 'Hey, these anons are saying bad things about us,' and you guys would be like, 'Who cares? Even if you think that, what is better in this world than the transfer of wealth from people who have a lot of money to people who have ideas and want to build something? Even if we're wrong about everything, that's still good for humanity.' So, shut up, you hater idiots. Haterade. And, as Erik knows, my response was: 'No, that's a great thing to get criticized for. Funding startups and supporting those startups is fantastic.'" There's a point within that which is also an important change in new media: you really want people to hate you and you want people to love you, but you don't want to be neutral. You don't want to be lukewarm, because then you're uninteresting. You can't be interesting and not have people both hate you and love you. No matter what you do, there's too much jealousy and haterade in the world for it not to be that way, because as soon as you get big, that's what happens. When people used to get upset about things written about us, I said, "Nobody writes a puff piece on Rupert Murdoch or Elon Musk anymore." It's never going to happen again. When you get to a certain size, people hate you, and that's good. It means you did it. You did something important and made a mark on the world. People care about what you're doing, and you have to take it that way. If you take it like, "Oh, I've got to stop doing that so people like me," you'll ruin your marketing. Yeah. Marc, you've since said the term: "They have all the right enemies." So, it's about picking the right people to hate you, which galvanizes the people who love you. What about building the right marketing and media team in the age of new media? What have you guys learned, or what advice do you have for CEOs in terms of making sure they get the right personality? What's a principle you think about for building that team? Well, the first principle is: if you're trained in old media, it's very, very hard to do new media. You have to be an exceptional person to make that transition. If you spent ten years doing old media, there are laws of physics, rules of the game, and things you do every single time—from vetting reporters to written Q&As to this and that. Everything about it is opposite world. There are very few people who can go, "Okay, I'm getting out of opposite world and going into new media world." You have to be careful, on the new media side, about hiring too much experience in non-new media. It looks like marketing, but it's not the same; it's a completely new skill set. That's probably the thing that I'd worry about the most. And then, what we looked for when we brought you on, Erik, for example, is: have you done it? Have you built some brand on some thing and some audience? Do you know how to build an audience? Because that's the core thing. If you can't do that, it doesn't matter if you've got this skill and that skill and the other skill—you have to be able to deliver it end-to-end. "Yeah, and in terms of people we've brought on—like Alex Danco, Henry, or Brent—they were product managers, founders, or investors, but they were obsessed with the discourse. They were listening to the podcasts, writing themselves; you could tell by the proof of work, even though they weren't officially doing the job." Yeah, storytelling on your new media team is such a good point. The reason why Alex is so good is because he's a world-class storyteller. Just take marketing out of it for a second: can you put together a story that somebody wants to read or listen to? That's a real skill. The elite level of that is way higher than the average level, and the people who can't do it can never do it. Many people from the old marketing world don't have that skill, because they relied on the principal or the CEO for the story. If you can build a team with multiple good storytellers, that's a huge power boost. By the way, there is a really big disconnect that goes back to the authenticity point. In the media sphere, every reporter listening to what we're saying would be like, "Oh, there are only two things: there's journalism and there's propaganda." They believe the mainstream, traditional press does journalism, and if you do direct communication, it's propaganda and "just marketing" trying to sell something. That is a distinction we 100% don't agree with. I don't think the press does much of what they would describe as objective journalism anymore. The positive side of it, which goes back to being authentic, is that you want people to actually understand who you are, what you do, and the context within which you are doing it. You want to explain yourself and explain the world. When we talk about storytelling, it's not a made-up story; it's storytelling of, "Here's what's actually happening," in an interesting way with tension, a beginning, and an end that keeps someone interested the whole way. "Yeah, that's right." As a consequence—and we really look for this—when Ben and I get stopped in the street, we routinely get told, "Wow, I watched the podcast, I saw this interview, I read that post." 100% of the time, they say, "That was great, I really understand what that topic is about." That is a very honest, legitimate, and worthwhile reaction to very honest, positive, and legitimate action on our part. I think this is a responsibility for people in our world and in tech to do. The changes happening in tech right now are profound, hard to understand from the outside, and incredibly complicated. There's a huge amount of noise in the environment, so explaining honestly what's actually going on is crucial. I'll just brag on our team: we put a SpaceX post up today, which, although it has my name on it, I had nothing to do with writing. I cannot take any credit for it, but the feedback we're getting is that it's the best thing anybody's ever written on SpaceX, and I think that's true. It literally lays out the actual truth, and I'm really proud of that. That's awesome. I want to close by going deeper on some advice for founders on going direct. Gabby, I'll start with you. What are some mistakes that you see CEOs making, and what are things to avoid? I would say there are two mistakes that I commonly saw working with founders, and they're sort of related. The first one is—and it can be very easy to do this, it's a trap to fall into because the timeline feels so addicting—but a lot of founders really over-index on distribution and tactics before actually getting the message right. Distribution is really just a multiplier on the message. If the message is wrong, now you've amplified something that is either irrelevant for your business, not what your audience needs to hear, or, worst of all, uninteresting. Now you've told everybody you care about that you're not very interesting. In practice, a lot of founders would come to us and say, "How do we go viral?" or "How do we get on Joe Rogan?" If you actually think about it, you could get on Joe Rogan and not say the right thing, which is maybe the worst thing ever. This is important because it applies not just to old media but to new media. You could go on Bloomberg or Fox and get the message wrong, and you could also do the same thing with new media or going direct. So, the first mistake is not actually spending the time to get the message right, which is the highest-leverage, most important thing to nail. From there, I guess the next mistake is actually figuring out how to do that. The way that I see it, you can get the message wrong if you focus too much on the inputs as opposed to the outputs or the outcomes that you want to drive towards. This is sort of paradoxical because the companies that are very successful struggle with this the most. There are a lot of interesting things that you could say about your company—so many milestones, the mission is compelling, there are different things you want to say—but depending on what you're trying to achieve, the thing you want to say should probably be different. Just because everything is true doesn't mean it's all relevant or strategic for your business. Instead of starting with the inputs of, "Here's this huge mess of everything we could say, let's just say it all right now and hope people remember the thing we think is most interesting," start with the outcome. Do we want to sell to a certain type of enterprise customer? Do we want to hire a certain type of engineer who believes a certain thing about us and our role in the market? Now you can work backward from there. We know who these people are, what they believe, what they need to believe about us, and what feels urgent, timely, and personal to them in the discourse. We can work backward and get the message right. Going back to point one, you pair that with really powerful distribution, and then you have a really winning strategy. A really important point in what Gabby said is that it determines how you have to put the team together. If you put together a team that you can't have a conversation with about your message and what you're saying, then you've done yourself a disservice. You need the team to help make the story great before they go out. It's really easy to say, "Okay, market this empty box." There are few marketers who can do that, and it's hard. You want the team to be invested in the message so all the variations you use to distribute that message are good and on point. If the team doesn't have an opinion on the message, that's very difficult. Marc, one of the things we said in the meeting with the growth portfolio company the other week was that what we admire about people like Alex Karp, Palmer, Elon, or others is the ability to not just focus on what they're doing, but also go outside-in. They talk about what's happening in the world and how they situate their worldview, their company, and their product within that. Why don't you talk a little bit about that principle and how people get good at it? Yeah, so the most common VC self-marketing story is: "Oh, I met this great founder, and we went on a walk, and then I called him eight times, and I thought he was great, and I eventually convinced him to take money." The most common startup story is: "Oh, we're a brand-new company, we're all fired up, we're ready to go, it's going to be great, we have a new product, and we hope you try it." Those stories just make you want to stab yourself in the neck, right? It's just like, "Oh my God, that is the lamest story in the world." "By the way, details—one of the keys on this is that good storytellers have great details. Details matter a lot, and the one thing about Marc's example that makes it so horrible is no details. Just blah, blah, blah, 'I'm so great, I talked to a founder, he really liked me, you'll probably like me too.' Who cares?" And it's almost this thing where they come across as fake humble, right? Because it's just like, 'Oh, just... oh, I'm humbly... I'm humbled by...' That's the most fake humble thing in the world. 'I'm humbled by...' You're not even remotely humbled. Yeah, and so it's just the worst. There's a whole bunch of critiques: it's fake bragging, it's boring, it's egocentric to no purpose, it's passive-aggressive, and then it's just indistinguishable. There's just a thousand of them. It's every single startup. Everybody can gaze at their navel, right? Everybody can do that. There's nobody who can't do that, so that's not a differentiated story. That's the default kind of narrative that people fall into. Then it also feels like doing anything bigger than that is arrogant. It's like, "Who are we to go tell some bigger thing? I don't even know what the bigger thing would be, and aren't the people who tell a bigger thing full of themselves and trying to make themselves look even bigger?" There is this real reluctance to expand the telescope out. But what I always tell people is that the exact opposite of all of that is true. The story of you and your startup is not inherently an interesting story, but there is almost certainly an interesting story that *involves* your startup. That story—and this is sort of the cheat code of it—is something else happening in the world that is incredibly interesting, which your company relates to. Yeah, by the way, the grand wizard of this is Alex Karp. If you watch his interviews, he never talks about Palantir. The only thing he ever says about Palantir—Marc pointed this out to me—is "ontology" and "orchestration," two words that nobody knows the meaning of. Nobody knows what Palantir does as a result, but it doesn't matter. Whether it's the future of the US military, superintelligence, or whatever the really good story is, Alex will go tell that story. "Neuro-divergent, I mean..." He's just like, whatever is interesting, he'll start talking about, and because he's the founder and CEO of Palantir, that just works. I say it kind of facetiously, but it's really a good strategy. Now, he takes it to the extreme, but that's the right idea: find the most interesting story you can plug your company into, and then tell that story. Because when something happens in the world—something involving the US military, AI in the military, geopolitics with China—he is the first phone call, right? Because he's the guy who's been out there talking about it. "By the way, Ryan Petersen, who's in the audience, has done a phenomenal job of that." Exactly, yes. World class, yes. The difference between talking about freight versus talking about the global supply chain completely collapsing during COVID, and how we're all going to starve to death, right? Therefore, he is the guy who literally goes on "60 Minutes" to explain to the world that, in fact, we are all about to starve to death—from a helicopter, right? "Yeah, from the helicopter, yes. 'Look at all these ships that are never landing, your children are about to starve.' By Flexport." It works incredibly well. And you don't even have to say "by Flexport"; it just happens. It's implicit. "It's implicit." Especially for enterprise sales, which is certainly Flexport but also Palantir, a big part of it is: are you important enough to meet with the CEO of your customer? Are you important enough to get in the room with the decision-maker? Are you important enough to meet with the Secretary of Defense, or be in the White House, or meet with Fortune 500 CEOs? Just saying, "I have a little startup that's doing interesting things" does not do that. But if you are attached to, and have been seen talking about, the big important things happening in the world and how they relate, and you potentially have an answer to it, that's absolute catnip. Everybody wants that. The trick is: don't think inside-out—me, my company, and my product out into the world. Don't think that way. Think in terms of what are the most interesting things happening in the world, and then how do those things relate to us. Yeah, and by the way, this is where CEOs go off the rails, because they're so focused on what they're doing that all they want to do is tell their story. They're not even paying attention to what's going on in the world, which is understandable because you're trying to build something. But to do the marketing exercise, investing in understanding what's happening out there is just critically important. You can't do it without that. This, by the way, happens also in investor relations. Every public company by law does these S-1, K-1, or Q-filings. They spend all this time and effort on these documents to fully explain everything the company does, with every possible hedge, mea culpa, and incredible description. But what percentage of the investors in Palantir have read the S-1 or K-1? Like, 0.0001 percent. What percentage have seen Alex on YouTube doing his thing? 100%. Look at the amount of time that goes into that document, right? So, you just reallocate that time and just understand enough about the world to put together a good story. On that note, we'll end with this idea that the skill set is not only something you are born with or you're not—it can be developed, learned, and cultivated. I mean, Marc, you were saying the other day that when you look back at Alex Karp's old interviews, they are very different. When you look at Alex, Palmer, Elon, Ryan, or Amjad, who came here earlier, and you look back at their old tweets or their old interviews, they're nowhere close to where they are now. "Oh, yeah, it's a skill set for sure." Look, there are people who are gifted, and then people who are less. If you look at Donald Trump's interviews in the '80s, they're very old-media interviews—he's actually restrained. And then, you know, he did figure out new media, and he's always super entertaining and interesting, which is kind of the magic of his popularity. So even at that level, you can develop it. "Yeah, well, on that note, it's a great place to wrap. So, please give a round of applause for Gabby, Ben, and Marc. Thank you."

30 Years Since the First Deposit: What Economy Will Young Norwegians Inherit?

Norges Bank Investment Management(123.0K)·87:59·938 views·2026-06-19
Jens StoltenbergMinister of Finance Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management Erlend MørchPresenter, NRK
TL;DRNorway’s Government Pension Fund Global (the Oil Fund) has grown from a 1.981 billion NOK deposit in 1996 to over 21,000 billion NOK today, now funding over 25% of the national budget.
As the country faces tighter fiscal conditions, the nation must balance maximizing...

TL;DR

Norway’s Government Pension Fund Global (the Oil Fund) has grown from a 1.981 billion NOK deposit in 1996 to over 21,000 billion NOK today, now funding over 25% of the national budget. As the country faces tighter fiscal conditions, the nation must balance maximizing financial returns with growing political pressures, ethical divestment demands, and a vital need to foster domestic labor and entrepreneurship.

The Evolution and Mechanics of the 21,000 Billion NOK Fund

  • The Foundation of Wealth: Minister of Finance Jens Stoltenberg detailed the three pivotal political choices that built the fund:
      • 100% Savings Rate: Norway decided by law to funnel 100% of state oil and gas revenues directly into the fund, backed by a high 78% marginal tax rate on oil companies.
      • The Fiscal Rule (*Handlingsregelen*): Established in 2001, this rule dictates that the government should only spend the real return of the fund (historically estimated at 3%, though currently kept at a safer 2.7% to 2.8% to build a buffer). This currently transfers nearly 600 billion NOK annually to the state budget.
      • The Shift to Equities: In 1997, Norway shifted from low-yield bonds to investing 40% of the fund in equities, increasing this share to 60% in 2007 and roughly 71% today. This transition is the single most profitable decision in Norwegian history.
  • More "Stock Fund" than "Oil Fund": Out of the total 21,000 billion NOK value, only 9,600 billion NOK stems from direct oil and gas revenues. An impressive 13,500 billion NOK has been generated solely through investment returns.
  • The Budget Lifeline: The fund’s returns now cover over 27% of all government spending—meaning more than one in four kroner spent on schools, healthcare, defense, and pensions is funded directly by these investment returns.

Investment Strategy, AI, and Global Real Estate

  • Growth Drivers: Nicolai Tangen, CEO of Norges Bank Investment Management (NBIM), noted that the fund grew from 10,000 billion NOK to 21,000 billion NOK during his tenure. Over 2,000 billion NOK of this growth was driven purely by the rise of major US tech companies.
  • The Reality of AI: Addressing concerns of an artificial intelligence bubble, Tangen noted that while valuations are high, the technological curve is exponential and adoption is still in its infancy globally. NBIM leverages AI extensively, including setting up specialized summer programs for graduates to work on AI initiatives.
  • Contrarian Investing Philosophy: Tangen emphasized that successful investing requires a willingness to think differently and act contrary to the crowd. He encouraged young people to cultivate unique, niche interests as a strength for financial careers.
  • Tangible Global Assets: To hedge risk, the fund maintains a massive real estate portfolio, which includes owning 25% of London’s Regent Street, alongside premium properties in Paris and Berlin, and significant logistics hubs.

The Ethical Dilemma: Financial Returns vs. Political Tool

  • The Debate on Divestment: A central conflict emerged during the youth politician panel regarding whether the fund should actively divest from controversial regions or companies:
      • Frøya Schjold (MDG) and Gaute Skjerve (AUF) argued that Norway has a moral obligation to divest from companies involved in human rights violations, specifically pointing to companies like Caterpillar for their machinery being used in Israeli settlements, and referencing the 2006 Walmart divestment and 2022 Russia exit as precedents.
      • Simen Velle (FrP) cautioned against turning the fund into an active geopolitical tool. He asserted that arbitrary divestments threaten financial returns and invite dangerous geopolitical scrutiny from powerful allies like the US, who may view unilateral actions negatively.
  • Defense and Double Standards: The panel debated the ethics of weapon investments. While the fund bans investments in chemical or nuclear weapons, Velle pointed out the contradiction in utilizing state funds to purchase hardware from defense giants like Lockheed Martin for national defense, while simultaneously debating whether the fund should profit from those very same entities.

Future Economic Outlook for Young Norwegians

  • Labor Over Oil: Stoltenberg delivered a stark warning that while the fund is massive, it represents only a fraction of Norway’s true wealth. The collective value of Norwegian labor is more than ten times greater than the value of the oil, gas, and the fund combined. Maintaining high workforce participation is crucial to avoiding the "oil curse."
  • The Entrepreneurship Gap: Simen Velle expressed deep concern that Norway's current tax system actively penalizes startup founders. He highlighted cases of young entrepreneurs relocating to Sweden (such as Stockholm) due to wealth taxes on paper valuations before companies even turn a profit, warning that Norway must "celebrate founders like we celebrate our national football team."
  • Social Equality and Housing: Youth representatives highlighted the critical domestic challenges awaiting future generations, specifically pointing to the difficulty of entering the housing market without wealthy parents and the rising number of highly educated graduates struggling to find relevant employment.

Notable Quotes

"Oil is important, but labor is much more important. When we look at what makes Norway wealthy, the value of the work we perform is more than ten times as valuable as all the oil, gas, and the pension fund combined." — Jens Stoltenberg, Minister of Finance
"In the stock market, you earn the most by doing the opposite of everyone else. If you have something that makes you a bit quirky and different, that is something you must cultivate." — Nicolai Tangen, CEO of Norges Bank Investment Management
"We have a draft of instruments to conduct peace work in the world... I don’t think the Oil Fund should be an instrument to create peace. I think the Oil Fund should be an instrument to generate as much money as absolutely possible for the Norwegian welfare state." — Simen Velle, FrP Representative

Dere skal ta ansvaret for det fondet, enten som politikere eller som økonomer eller som jurister, kanskje som jobber med fondet, eller bare som borgere i Norge som stemmer på partier som skal passe på dette fondet også i framtiden. Så derfor er jeg, ja, ekstra glad for å være her i dag fordi vi snakker om noe viktig.
