TL;DRSEC Commissioner Hester Peirce highlights a pivotal shift toward digital asset integration, emphasizing the SEC’s proposed elimination of the 20-year-old Rule 611 and the development of an "innovation exemption" to facilitate controlled, on-chain financial experimentation.

TL;DR

SEC Commissioner Hester Peirce highlights a pivotal shift toward digital asset integration, emphasizing the SEC’s proposed elimination of the 20-year-old Rule 611 and the development of an "innovation exemption" to facilitate controlled, on-chain financial experimentation. As she prepares to transition out of her role, Peirce urges regulators to maintain an open mind toward innovation while fiercely protecting user privacy, self-custody, and developer freedom.

***

Reconsidering Rule 611 and Modernizing Market Structure

  • The Rule 611 Proposal: The SEC has proposed a rule to eliminate Rule 611 (the "trade-through rule"), a 20-year-old equity market regulation.
      • Historical Context: In 2005, current Chairman Atkins and Commissioner Cynthia Glassman issued a 44-page written dissent against the implementation of Rule 611.
      • The Trade-Through Mechanism: Designed to prevent trading through a better price, the rule mandates that transactions occur at or improve upon the national best bid or offer (NBBO).
  • Obsolete Technology: Peirce argues that while the rule may have been conceived when venues like the New York Stock Exchange lacked modern technology, today's highly interconnected, high-tech markets no longer require it.
      • The Role of Arbitrage: Market participants and arbitrageurs naturally bind fragmented marketplaces together without the need for rigid regulatory mandates.
      • Implications for Tokenization: Removing Rule 611 will reduce market fragmentation and complex order types, creating a friendlier environment for future asset tokenization.

Dispelling Myths Around the "Innovation Exemption"

  • Current Status: Peirce clarifies that the highly anticipated "innovation exemption" has not yet been officially released, despite various media reports and speculation.
  • Exclusion of Synthetics: The framework will not apply to synthetic securities (where an SPV holds an asset and grants investors economic exposure without direct ownership). Synthetics must still go through traditional U.S. registration processes.
  • On-Chain Finance vs. DeFi: The exemption is designed for permissioned, "on-chain finance" rather than fully decentralized finance (DeFi).
      • Controlled Experimentation: The goal is to allow market participants to conduct real-world, limited-scale experiments to prove the viability of tokenized securities.
      • Regulatory Insights: This trial period gives regulators a window to observe how tokenized trading operates and identify correct points of regulatory intervention.
  • Smart Contract Utility: Bringing securities on-chain enables programmable features, such as embedding transfer restrictions for company founders, simplifying collateral management, facilitating investor communication via NFTs, and combating naked short selling.

Driving Capital Formation and Encouraging Earlier IPOs

  • The Problem of Delayed IPOs: Companies are staying private much longer—sometimes raising up to "Series M" private funding rounds—which deprives retail investors of early-stage wealth generation.
  • Public Market Benefits: Public listings provide retail investors with tested valuations and transparent disclosures, while giving companies acquisition capital and employee liquidity.
  • Mitigating Disincentives to Go Public:
      • Disclosure Requirements: The SEC is evaluating redundant disclosure requirements that add cost burden without providing useful information to investors.
      • Shareholder Litigation: The SEC is staying neutral on companies and shareholders opting for arbitration over traditional litigation to resolve disputes.
      • Analyst Coverage: The commission is exploring ways to make it easier for companies to obtain research analyst coverage to support healthy trading volumes.

SEC-CFTC Harmonization and Regulatory Priorities

  • Joint Agency Collaboration: The SEC and CFTC are actively working together to establish clear jurisdictions for novel products, such as perpetual contracts (perps), to ensure market participants know where and how to list them.
  • Operational "Nuts and Bolts": The SEC’s current focus includes defining the role of broker-dealers, establishing guidelines for transfer agents interacting with blockchains, regulating custody on both the broker-dealer and investment adviser sides, and addressing clearing agency requirements.
      • The DTCC Initiative: The DTCC was recently granted a no-action letter by SEC staff to experiment with blockchain-based settlement systems.
  • Peirce's Departure: Peirce’s official term ended last June, and though she can stay until the end of the year, she will be departing early to teach law school. She expresses strong confidence that Chairman Atkins will maintain a fast, pro-innovation regulatory pace.

Protecting Core Principles: Privacy, Self-Custody, and Developer Freedom

  • Non-Negotiable Pillar Principles: Peirce emphasizes that future statutory and regulatory frameworks must respect three foundational tenets:
      • Self-Custody: Individuals must retain the right to self-custody their own digital assets.
      • Privacy: The financial transaction privacy of innocent citizens must be safeguarded.
      • Developer Freedom: Software developers must retain the right to write and publish open-source code without needing prior government permission.
  • A Layered Approach to Privacy and Security: Peirce suggests balancing illicit-finance monitoring with consumer privacy by maintaining a "credibly neutral" base blockchain layer.
      • Application-Level Compliance: Regulatory features—such as asset freezes, identity verification, or transaction reversals—should be built on the application layer rather than forcing privacy compromises at the protocol level.

***

Notable Quotes

"We really need to look at this as protecting the privacy of innocent people's transactions should be very important to us... We just can't leave the privacy of innocent people on the cutting room floor." — Hester Peirce
"Build things that people want and need and then it will be the kind of thing that is more likely to stay around because people want and need them, and so they'll speak up if a regulator tries to take them away." — Hester Peirce
"It's on-chain finance. It's not decentralized finance, it's on-chain finance... What we're trying to do is allow people to do controlled experiments using on-chain finance to help the market think through whether tokenization makes sense." — Hester Peirce

And so that's a project that has many different pieces to it, but that's something we're working on. Just yesterday we proposed a rule that would eliminate Rule 611, which is the trade-through rule in equity markets. And that is a 20-year-old rule that I think was really ripe for reconsideration, so I'm excited about that. But I think it will be quite helpful for the industry to have this legislative framework within which we and the CFTC are writing rules. Yep, and I think that that is a great place to start with the recent 611 proposal to remove that piece of regulation. I was reading a thread about this just a little while ago and it turns out that, I guess Chairman Atkins, this has kind of been 20 years in the making. In 2005, Chairman Atkins and Commissioner Cynthia Glassman issued a 44-page written dissent on this vote that put 611 into place. And so, why don't we start here. There's ways in which this is protecting the national best bid and offer as it pertains to trading. Some of these are fundamentally essentially impossible with some of the AMM technical structures that are available with exchanges on-chain. And so it feels like this piece of regulation is a bit antiquated. But before we draw that conclusion, could you describe to us what Rule 611 is, and then we can get into, you know, whether it makes sense or it doesn't make sense in the digital on-chain age? Yeah, I mean the rule came from the concern that there were different marketplaces that were not being united, and so you might not be getting the best price that you should, that you could get if you were trading at some other venue. And so the idea is that, in order for a trade to go through, it has to either be at the national best bid or offer—so that's like the best price that's out there—or you have to improve on it. Otherwise, it can't—that trade can't go through. So it's called the trade-through rule because it doesn't allow you to trade through a better price. My view is that markets don't need a rule to bind them together. That's what arbitrageurs do; that's what markets are all about. If you see a good deal in one place and it's a worse deal in another, market participants will figure out a way to even that out. And so that was my concern at the time that the rule was initially adopted. In any event, even if you thought that it was necessary at that time, that was a time where a lot of—the New York Stock Exchange, for example, wasn't using the most modern technology. And so since then everyone has upgraded to extremely modern technology. And so there are a lot of interconnections between markets now; they're all hooked together. And so I think people, even people who might have thought it was a good idea then, are questioning whether it still is. We just put it out for proposal, so we'll be getting good comments and I'm excited about that. But I think we've seen some things happen like the fragmentation of the marketplace, lots of different exchanges, and lots of different complex order types, and some of that we believe to be tied to the fact that this rule exists. So, yes, I think it could be helpful as we move toward tokenization not to have this kind of rule in place, but that isn't really what was driving the proposal. Got it. Okay. And you know, I read a little bit of commentary even in the last 24 hours that talks about, you know, how the current structure of some of the on-chain exchanges aren't necessarily prepared to support national best bid and offer. But as you're saying, you make the case that even in off-chain exchanges—you mentioned, you know, the New York Stock Exchange, Nasdaq—these sorts of things are also innovating, and they're using upgraded technology. Even in that case, the markets support maybe a more sophisticated approach that doesn't regulate that national best bid and offer. That helps these markets ultimately find their fair market price and create an efficient market. And so, as we're thinking through, you know, some of the market structure upgrades, I think people are looking at the tokenized equities market structure and they're thinking that this could fundamentally reshape how these things are traded and transacted. I think generally we're all in support of tokenization. There's some market structure changes that are coming. And tokenized equities and the innovation exemption is one of these that is ultimately going to put some of these changes in place. Maybe you could just kind of dispel some of the misconceptions around the innovation exemption. Well, so first, the innovation exemption has not yet been released. So, you know, I know there've been people writing news stories and things like that, but it's not—it's not actually out there yet. So that's one myth that should be dispelled. The second is some people have talked about it as if it's going to be used to support the trading of synthetic securities. So securities—and we put out a tokenization—not framework, but sort of a—the staff put out a taxonomy, so you can—there are three categories and one of those is synthetic securities where you're getting exposure to a security but you don't actually hold the security. Those would not be, in my mind, were not ever something that we were thinking about including in the innovation exemption. Now someone could come and register those kinds of securities in the United States if they wanted to, but that wasn't, sort of as I had been thinking about the innovation exemption, wasn't something I had been thinking about. So the idea is to allow tokens to trade on-chain, basically. So it's on-chain finance. It's not decentralized finance; it's on-chain finance. There's still permissioning involved. Basically, what we're trying to do is allow people to do controlled experiments using on-chain finance to help us—to help, obviously, the market think through whether tokenization makes sense, see what it looks like to try to do this not fully at scale but at least to be able to try it in the real world. But also, it will allow us as the regulator an opportunity to see how this works and to think about where the proper points of regulation are. There are going to be some differences with the way the traditional financial world works, and we want to make sure that we're applying regulation at the right points. So I think people should look at it as an opportunity for everyone to really explore trading of tokenized securities. We don't see a lot of tokenized securities yet, and I expect that we will. I think there are some advantages to being able to have things trading on-chain. I think there are advantages to being able to put a smart contract with an asset. It's very powerful in a lot of ways and I think could be helpful to meeting regulatory requirements, could be helpful to, you know, figuring out—I think having securities on-chain could be helpful for people who want to use them as collateral. There are a lot of potential reasons that it could be useful. But until we allow people to do some experimentation with trading, I don't see that people are going to move to tokenization. So this really, I view as a step toward allowing folks to imagine what the future will look like. You make a great point that in the early stages of this, it looks like on-chain finance but not necessarily decentralized finance. I think there are some DeFi purists that may take issue with that. But ultimately I'm very optimistic that this is a stepping stone, if you will, into a more innovative environment where we're using the best technology. You bring up some great use cases of smart contracts, using these things for collateral, using them for compliance or regulatory purposes that smart contracts enable us to do, but not necessarily a decentralized environment. It's still a permissioned environment. I think there's some people that would argue with us, but I would tend to agree with you that there is a value proposition there. Maybe there's another value proposition of extending this even further into a decentralized environment, but that is when we start breaching into even further unknowns, and it remains to be seen how we best get there. So thinking about those kinds of issues will also be a big focus. Certainly, and this is part of the foundation that you lay for the following commissioners that come after. And so, in addition to the rules that we've talked about today, I'm curious: Are there criteria or processes that you've seen companies or, you know, entities take now that you think are the way things should be done? You know, what in general can these entities take into account that will serve as the building blocks going into the future? For instance, you mentioned the DTCC got this no-action letter. Obviously they are a very prominent entity in this entire industry. Is there any general criteria or processes that you would recommend as we move forward that you anticipate will become sustainable in the regulation moving forward? I mean, the biggest thing I would recommend is think about what you're trying to do, what you need to do to make that commercially viable, and then what kind of regulatory relief you need. But this is a very complicated legal area and people really need to be thinking about: Do I need to come in and register this product? Do I need to ask for no-action relief from something? Might I be pulled into the definition of broker-dealer, investment adviser, or investment company? Don't panic if that's the case. Come in and talk to us, and we can think about what makes sense in terms of what would it look like for you to register your product. Are there any requirements that don't make sense because we have information on the blockchain that maybe replicates information that would otherwise be generated through a regulatory requirement? So I think really best practices are to get good—I hate to tell people that they have to go talk to lawyers, but go talk to a good lawyer who specializes in this area. And then, really, we—the crypto task force, but more generally the staff at the SEC—are very eager to engage, and I think we can make some good progress. It's only by people being willing to come in and say, "Hey, this is what I'm trying to do, I think we need some relief here," that we can move things forward. Yeah, and this is a relatively recent phenomenon. I mean, I'm sure you can remember the SEC wasn't always as open-minded to some of the innovations that are happening in the digital asset industry. That's no longer the case, but I think that is somewhat still a precedent in the industry's mind. And that has changed, and you've been a pioneer and a leader as it pertains to that change. Well, yeah, although I will say, and I'm very grateful for people who are coming in and talking to us. And I, you know, I talk to companies that say, "We're trying to do this, but we need to go to market soon. We can't just wait and wait and wait." And so, I feel that pressure, and I would love to be able to move things along faster. And so there is a bit of an apology here, too, that things do take a long time. Well, no need to apologize. I think you've been such a, you know, a lightning bolt as it pertains to the regulatory state of our industry, at least relative to some of the previous regulatory complexes and representatives in the SEC. And I guess my question here is: How do we maintain this pace? Because the SEC is very open-minded and willing to collaborate with industry partners and establish best practices. Some of these definitions are starting to become formed in the taxonomy. And so you've accelerated the pace of regulatory change in the SEC in this current regime. As you transition out, how do we ensure that we don't go backwards and we continue to accelerate the pace of the regulatory regime? Well, I think as long as Chairman Atkins is here, the pace will continue. He's very committed to getting clear rules in place and, you know, he's someone who's spent a lot of time thinking about what good financial regulation looks like. So he has a real appreciation for the need for people to be able to do things that are commercially viable, but also he has a high commitment to achieving the goals of the commission, which are protecting investors, facilitating capital formation, and fostering market integrity. So I think that piece will be fine as long as he's here. I would also say—and this is the message I give everywhere and all the time, and so it's a bit of a repeat—but build things that people want and need, and then it will be the kind of thing that is more likely to stay around because people want and need them, and so they'll speak up if a regulator tries to take them away. Yeah, yeah, that makes complete sense, and we're seeing those products come to market in real-time. Another thing that we, you know, are very, very excited about, as we've been talking about, is this innovation exemption. I just wanted to double-click on it for the sake of—we've talked about the Clarity Act as well and how we kind of understand the typical process of how this becomes more established. You know, it passes the House, passes the Senate, goes to the President's desk, he signs it, and it becomes a law. When it comes to the proposed innovation exemption, how does this progress? Ultimately, who are the stakeholders that have to sign off on this before it becomes established and concrete in the regulation? Existing laws already give us the ability to create exemptions. We can do that by rule or by order. And so it's a commission decision; it doesn't require input from Congress because they already gave us the permission to do this. So it's really an internal process. But as we discussed before, there are a lot of complicated issues here and we want to make sure that we get it right, so that's what we're working on. Awesome. And as we think about some of the foundations that are being laid and moving forward, you mentioned sort of the classifications of tokenized securities. They may fall into this tokenized directly by the issuer or on their behalf, and then this third party. We're in the midst of this mega IPO season; as we're recording this, SpaceX went and went live just hours ago. And so this has been another catalyst in this area of tokenized equities, and there's all kinds of SPV wrappers around some of these company shares. How can we think about the third-party tokenization? Because that's generally kind of one of the two classifications here, and we've been talking about innovation exemptions primarily for that first class of tokenized equities on behalf of the issuer themselves. Well, there are actually three categories. So I point people back to the staff statement, and one is natively tokenized, the second is you can have an entitlement where there's actually a share being custodied on behalf and the token represents that, and then you've got the third category which is this synthetic category. And so, yeah, with respect to the third category, that's one where again people can register those kinds of securities, but it's important for people who hold securities that way to understand that they're getting economic exposure to the asset but they're not actually holding the asset itself. And so that's the piece that, you know, if you were to register something like that in the United States, you would have to really explain that to your investors. Right. And so you had put out a post again trying to clarify some of these misconceptions where, you know, you appreciated the interest in but not the hyperbole of the innovation exemption and as it pertains to some of these classifications. Was there something in particular that you had intended where maybe some of the more egregious examples of things that people were claiming was effectively around some of this third-party tokenization and there was this maybe broad exemption for those? But you've made it clear that's not the case. And so could you just explain a little bit more, based on the feedback that you've gotten on this proposed exemption so far, how narrow or broad you would anticipate this exemption when it ultimately becomes processed? I mean, again, it's not out, but as I said, I had never envisioned it as involving that third category, which is synthetics, which are securities held in an SPV but then someone can get economic exposure to the security through that SPV. That's not what I had been thinking. So again, I may not be here when the innovation exemption comes out, so I think it's important to say it's a conception, and so we'll have to see what the final looks like. But I think there was a lot of hyperbole about arguing that it's much broader than it is. And as you said, some people are going to be disappointed because it's actually pretty traditional, right? It's on-chain finance. It's a step to see how tokenized securities will trade. It is not, you know, it's not even that big of a step, frankly. I think it's important, but it's not that big of a step. Right. Okay. And ultimately, there's obviously protocols that have been developing the rails for on-chain finance for a long time. They've innovated into that more decentralized direction. But we've also got the incumbent institutions that have been here and they harness the lion's share of securities trading, and they're also now coming into the on-chain world. And this is a step for them to do that. We've thought about the New York Stock Exchange alternative trading system and how some of these structures are ultimately coming to market. The DTCC is also a big part of this. And so, obviously part of the SEC's mandate is to protect investors as these things come online. And so, the tokenization of these securities—is it primarily for making these markets more efficient? You also mentioned some of the regulatory benefits of tokenizing these securities, bringing them into smart contracts on-chain. Could you talk about what that looks like in practice, maybe some of the more novel smart contract constructions that you may have seen or heard about or thought about, and how they can benefit both institutions and newer companies alike as we tokenize more of these securities? Well, I think that when we think about tokenization, it's really just a new format for securities. So you can have paper certificates, you can have dematerialized, immobilized—that's what we have now, certificates, so you're not actually holding the paper. Or this is another option: tokenization, because it does allow things to run on the same rails that your stablecoins will be running on, and that could be efficient and effective, I think, for using tokenized securities in a variety of ways. One of them is you may want to post it as collateral; you may want to lend out your securities. It'll make it easier for you to lend out your securities, and that's an area where you can make a little bit of a return. You—I think we'll see an advantage to being able to program securities. So if you're a founder of the company and we want to put restrictions on your sales, we can do that through a smart contract. I think there also will be interesting possibilities for companies to communicate with their investors by dropping an NFT in the investor's wallet. They won't necessarily even need to know who the investor is, but they'll be able to communicate. That could have some implications for voting as well. And then I think we'll see for companies that have treasuries, for them to do treasury management. If they have tokenized money market funds, it will be easier than if they're not tokenized to be more precise about it—they can lend it for part of the day and then they can take it back. It will allow for more flexibility, I should say. And then I think there's also a possibility that it could be helpful in combating naked short selling. So that's when you're a short seller, you need to locate the shares ahead of time, and so this will be a way to hold people to account for actually doing that. So I see a number of different possibilities. Again, I'm just a regulator and I really am excited to see what the market decides it wants to do. But I think there are a lot of possibilities there. Yep. And we've seen a lot of demand for these things both in, you know, maybe the traditional financial world as well as some of this next-generation on-chain financial world as well. I'm a little bit more curious to chat about the IPO season that we're kind of seeing unfold before us. You mentioned at the top of the conversation how you'd like to see companies go public a little bit sooner. I think I just saw a company raising like a Series M, which I had never heard about before. And so, I guess just generally to start: Why is it important that we broaden access for these companies and we have them go public sooner? And what steps are important to ultimately incentivize the companies for going public rather than maintaining shareholder value internally and staying private for longer? What are some of the steps that we can take to incentivize this? Well, it's important because retail investors are going to get most of their access to the markets through the public markets. And we can and are planning to take steps that will make it easier for them to participate in the private markets. But realistically, public markets are a great place for retail investors to get access. There's a lot of information out there about those companies, the valuations are tested by the public markets, and that's a good thing. And it can be very good for companies, too, because it enables them to give liquidity to their employees and it enables them to use their shares as acquisition capital to go buy other companies. So there are a lot of benefits to companies as well. The thing that we're looking at is why are companies deciding to stay private? Well, one is a good reason: they can find a lot of capital in the private markets. That's great, I don't want to stop that from happening. But there are some negative associations with being public. One is that you're required to make disclosures about a whole range of things, many of which are not important to your investors. I hear all the time: "The SEC requires these disclosures, our investors never ask us about them, we spend a lot of time putting these disclosures together, we don't get one question from investors about them." Well, that seems to me maybe that's an area where we could pull back some of the disclosure requirements. Or you get boilerplate disclosures that build up over time. And so we're looking at that. Shareholder litigation is one reason companies don't like to go public. We've tried to take some steps to at least make it possible for companies that decide they want to use arbitration instead to do that. That's up to the company and their shareholders, not up to us, and we just want to say we're not going to put our thumb on the scale one way or the other there. And then research analyst coverage is another area where companies, if they don't get that research analyst coverage, often don't trade as well, so we tried to take some steps to make it easier for companies to get covered by research analysis. And then we're looking at a range of other possibilities. Is there anything that we can do to make it easier for exchanges to experiment with how securities trade? So we're open to other ideas. I think there are a lot of factors that go into whether a company decides to go public or stay public, but I do think we have a role to play and we're trying to fulfill that. Absolutely. And I think we've seen this in the digital asset market, and these companies are choosing to go in a different direction than typically had been done. And so for the sake of universal market access, getting retail investors access to these companies that ultimately form the backbone of the economy is very, very important. One of the ways in which we've seen this, because ultimately these investors want access and exposure to the upside of these companies as they continue to scale, we've seen these pre-IPO markets become available. Not necessarily to US customers, but they are happening offshore. This is also involved in the PERPS landscape, which I understand is under the purview primarily of the CFTC. Do you think that under some of the innovative regulatory changes that you're looking at and considering, there's validity to these pre-IPO markets? I think one of them had it pretty with the SpaceX IPO today—the price discovery was relatively consistent with where the IPO inevitably launched. But do you think there's validity here? I'm just curious if you think that there's a path maybe for these to come and become a primary vehicle for retail investors to get exposure to these companies pre-IPO. I mean, I think what you're describing is there's a real retail appetite to get access to these companies earlier in their growth cycle. And that's why my preference is that we work on figuring out ways to encourage companies to go public earlier, and to say: "This is a good framework. We are trying to create a framework that gets investors the information that they need, but also that doesn't burden companies so that they don't want to go public." There are other alternatives out there; as you say, many of them are available overseas to get economic exposure to companies. If things like that are to trade in the United States, they would have to go through the processes required in the US. If you want to sell a security, it has to either be registered or subject to an exemption, so people would have to figure out how to comply with that. And then in terms of perpetual markets, I mean, they're an interesting market, an interesting product. As the CFTC is looking at them and trying to figure out how to bring that market onshore, that's interesting to watch. Some of those could potentially be within our remit and so that's an area where, as I talked about the CFTC-SEC harmonization, one of the things we're trying to do is make sure that everyone's on the same page about where products are regulated. And what does the harmonization look like in practice, maybe using perpetuals as an example? Given that these things are relatively new, maybe they don't fit as cleanly into the regulatory structure of a security or a commodity or a future. How do you and the CFTC go about analyzing some of these new technologies, ultimately dissecting it, putting some clear definitions? How have you been able to find harmony with the CFTC whereas other regulatory regimes haven't? Well, so work in progress. I mean, we're doing a lot of joint work to think through these issues, to look at different products and discuss them. But we're also looking for people to help us do that, and so we're looking for feedback from the public and we welcome that feedback. And I think you'll see that there are a lot of opportunities to come in and weigh in on that process. So if folks have a product that they are trying to launch and they want to know where does it fit, we do welcome specific requests also. Absolutely. Commissioner Peirce, I think I'd love to just kind of understand as maybe a longer-term view on, given that the tokenized equities are progressing, tokenization in general is progressing and you're sort of looking at the horizon and riding off into the sunset as we approach here your tenure, how do you think that this space in general is going to—what is your hope for how you'd like to leave the crypto task force, Project Crypto? These have been very, very strong initiatives. And as you transition out, what are some of the things that you hope are carried about? And how can we ensure that these are carried through in the coming regulatory regimes? Well, I hope that we'll just keep an open mind about innovation and about the possibility of improvements in the financial world that we regulate. I think often regulators can get very comfortable with the status quo because we know how it works, so we sort of stick with it the way it is. So an open-mindedness I think is important. I'm not concerned about that with Chairman Atkins; he is very open-minded to thinking about how new technologies fit within the existing regulatory scheme and he's open to the idea of adjusting rules where needed. I think that I'm not too worried about how things will be after I leave, because there's real momentum here at the CFTC, in Congress, and at the banking regulators to really think about these issues and to try to come up with rules that are appropriate and make sense. So I think that it's something that I look forward to watching from the outside. You know, I think there are going to be areas that people debate about, and I hope that we will respect certain basic principles. The ability of people to self-custody is very important; that should not be compromised. The ability of people to protect their privacy in their financial transactions is very important and should be protected. The ability of developers to develop code and put it out there without having to get permission from someone to do so should be protected, too. And so these kind of principles, I think, are really important. We obviously have seen a lot of hacks and bad activity, and I think it's in everyone's interest to try to prevent bad actors from taking advantage of the technology for their purposes. And I love seeing industry-led, grassroots initiatives to combat some of those kinds of things, and that's important. But really, for me, the biggest concern is that we will, as we draft these rules and statutes, respect those really core principles, and I think we will find that that will put us in a better place at the end of the day. One thing that comes to mind, because I realize how important it is to you and through the work that you've done, is the privacy element. We've seen stablecoin providers in particular have a bit of—well, frankly, they tend to have these seizures or freezes of these assets which are totally compliant, and that's in order to stay in compliance with the law. I guess the question here is: What are some of the best practices that maybe we can leave so that the privacy of some of these protocols is still respected, but so is the law, and we're not, you know, kind of laxadaisical as it pertains to potentially illicit movement of funds? This is a very tricky balance, but with your experience, you're very knowledgeable through your work at the SEC. Do you have any lessons learned or experiences that you can share as far as how you've been able to balance this for the regimes to follow? I think they're difficult questions and it is a balance, and you do have to make some decisions about what you're trying to protect. But we've done that in other instances, right? And so keeping the base layer neutral is a good way to, I think, start. You can build on top of it things that are consistent with regulatory requirements, or you can design them for all kinds of reasons. You can make things more centralized or less centralized, and I think there are applications for which decentralization makes a lot of sense. You might find other applications where you want to have some sort of an intermediary with a freezing function, with the ability to reverse transactions. So I think one of the advantages of the technology is it does allow some sort of this layering. But I think my message that I really have learned as a regulator is it's not a perfect world, and there are some choices that we're going to make that we make because we're a free people. And it's wonderful that there are folks out there who are really good at tracking down bad actors, and maybe their financial transactions, their transactions on-chain, are part of that helping to find them. But, you know, we really need to look at this as: protecting the privacy of innocent people's transactions should be very important to us, at the same time as it's also important to us to try to track down the bad guys. And I think if we put those two things out there as very important objectives, you'll find a lot of creativity in the private sector and in the public sector in achieving those two things. But we just can't leave the privacy of innocent people on the cutting room floor. That may seem like the right answer, but I think there are other ways to get to the right outcome. Absolutely. Yeah, look, it is a challenging problem, but we have not backed down as a country from challenging problems in the past. We've got a lot of very smart builders here and regulators here. And so, ultimately, we want the best of both worlds: we want privacy for innocent people, but we also want to catch any potential illicit activity. And I think you mentioned a very thoughtful construction about a credibly neutral base layer and applications on top that give users the freedom of choice, how much privacy that they're looking for, and also gives us flexibility in how we are protecting against some of those bad actors. So that makes complete sense. Obviously, I can tell that this is something that's very important and you've thought a lot about. And you know, just I guess the last question here is: As you think about what the future entails, you mentioned public sector, private sector—where are you going next? Is it, you know, are you going to stay in the public sector? We've seen a lot of regulators move from the public sector to the private sector. Just curious, about your passion for privacy and market access, is there an opportunity that lies ahead that you're excited about? Yeah, I'm going to be teaching law school, so I'm excited about working with the next generation. And I, you know, I always look at what we've done and we've left a lot of problems for the next generation to solve. And so I want to be part of preparing people who will grab those problems and fix them. Well, there's not a shortage of work to do, but you've done so much great work for the industry up to this point and will continue through the end of your tenure. So thank you so much for all of the great work and also for spending some time with us today. It is a very busy week and day here in the industry. So thank you so much, as always, and hopefully we can get you back for one more session before the end of your tenure there at the SEC. Well, thanks so much for having me.

Industrial AI in Action | Roland Busch Keynote | VivaTech 2026 Paris

Siemens(33.2K)·45:02·1.6K views·2026-06-19
Roland BuschCEO, Siemens
TL;DRAt VivaTech 2026, Siemens and its partners demonstrated how Industrial AI is transforming physical infrastructure from design through operations.
To scale successfully, companies must move past isolated pilot programs, establish a unified data foundation, and collaborate...

TL;DR

At VivaTech 2026, Siemens and its partners demonstrated how Industrial AI is transforming physical infrastructure from design through operations. To scale successfully, companies must move past isolated pilot programs, establish a unified data foundation, and collaborate to bypass critical deployment hurdles.

Redefining Design and Simulation with Digital Twins

  • Physics-Based Digital Twins: Siemens software allows companies to construct real-time, 3D digital replicas of machines, factories, and logistics networks to stress-test designs virtually before physical construction.
      • *Case Study:* Logistics giant Kion uses Siemens' digital twin composer to simulate how different warehouse layouts impact vehicle throughput before moving physical shelves.
  • Aerodynamics Acceleration: Combining Siemens simulation software with Nvidia’s accelerated computing speeds up highly complex BMW wind tunnel simulations by a factor of 10x to 1,000x.
  • Simcenter Simsolid Launch: Announced at VivaTech, this new simulation software bypasses the traditional, weeks-long meshing process. It runs structural, thermal, fatigue, and vibration calculations directly from designs in minutes—marking a 30x increase in speed.
  • Startup Empowerment Program: Siemens announced a startup program offering up to 95% discounts on its design, engineering, and simulation software suites.
      • *Case Study:* French rocket propulsion startup Latitude adopted the Siemens software suite, accelerating project delivery by six months and increasing engineering efficiency by 15%.

Accelerating Production and Automation via Industrial Copilot

  • Autonomous Engineering Agents: The Siemens Industrial Copilot operates end-to-end within the TIA Portal engineering platform. Unlike simple text chatbots, this agent writes, compiles, and validates PLC automation code autonomously.
      • *Real-World Impact:* Early adopters report a 50% increase in productivity, 2.5x faster development cycles, and 80% higher code quality.
  • New VivaTech Capabilities:
      • *Electrical Design Integration:* The Copilot can now ingest electrical design schematics and automatically map them into the TIA Portal, saving days of manual work.
      • *Compliance to Company Standards:* The agent is trained to read hundreds of pages of internal company standards to construct compliant software architectures automatically.
  • Physics AI on the Factory Floor: At Siemens' Erlangen facility—the world’s first fully AI-driven production site—100 active AI algorithms have yielded a 40% faster time-to-market, 42% lower energy use, and a 69% increase in productivity.
      • *Flexible Handling:* Erlangen uses Physics AI to train robotic arms to grip and package unpredictably-shaped objects (such as plastic bags of screws) that conventional automation cannot handle cost-effectively.

Overcoming the Three Traps of Industrial AI Adoption

Siemens Board Member Cedric Neike and Capgemini CEO Aiman Ezzat highlighted the "industrial AI syndromes" that keep companies stuck in perpetual trial phases:

  • The "Spray and Pray" Syndrome: Organizations appoint a Chief AI Officer and launch dozens of fragmented pilots without clear strategic focus. Neike joked that European companies often "have more pilots than Lufthansa or Air France."
  • The "Foundation" Syndrome: Companies fail to link their data islands. Siemens addresses this by forcing a unified "data fabric" across CRM, PLM, and ERP systems to make operational data accessible.
  • The "Lonesome Cowboy" Syndrome: Attempting to build and scale proprietary AI architectures in isolation. Scalability requires deep collaboration with systems integrators and technology partners.
  • Agentic Business Processes: Capgemini's Ezzat noted that modern enterprises are blending human workforces with "AI agents" (digital workers). This shift requires strict governance and control planes to prevent organizational chaos and run-away token consumption.

Real-World Case Studies in Heavy Industry and Pharma

  • GravitHy Green Steel (Marseille, France):
      • *The Project:* A €2.2 billion green steel investment aiming to replace coal with hydrogen to produce 2 million tons of steel annually.
      • *AI Application:* Capgemini and Siemens built a digital twin and an agentic AI layer for the plant. The AI serves as a real-time learning control brain that dynamically optimizes hydrogen consumption and manages predictive maintenance.
  • Sanofi Smart Operations:
      • *The Challenge:* Replicating and scaling drug manufacturing processes manually across 100 production lines and 50 global sites is highly prone to error.
      • *AI Application:* Implementing an electronic Manufacturing Execution System (MES) to digitize batch records and guide operators step-by-step.
      • *Results:* The project achieved a 70% reduction in batch times and an 80% decrease in process deviations, helping combat "Eroom's Law" (the trend of drug development costs doubling every nine years).

AI Infrastructure, ASML Collaboration, and European Policy

  • Direct Current (DC) AI Factories: Partnering with Nvidia, Siemens developed a blueprint for AI data centers operating entirely on direct current (DC). This links solar roofs, batteries, and GPUs directly to eliminate AC-to-DC conversion power losses.
      • *Fault Protection:* Siemens introduced a solid-state circuit breaker that cuts DC power faults in milliseconds—1,000x faster than mechanical switches—protecting millions of dollars in GPU clusters.
  • The ASML Partnership: ASML’s extreme ultraviolet (EUV) lithography machines contain over 20,000 highly customized parts. ASML utilizes Siemens’ Teamcenter software as its primary data backbone to track bills of material across design, manufacturing, and servicing.
  • European Competitiveness and Regulation: ASML CEO Christophe Fouquet and Siemens' Roland Busch discussed the role of the "Tech Creators" industry group. They urged European policymakers to regulate consumer AI and industrial machine data differently, warning that over-regulation threatens to widen the technological gap with the US and China.

<blockquote>"When AI enters a physical system, it stops being a feature, it becomes a force. A force with direct real-world impact... Hallucination is not acceptable in Industrial AI." — Roland Busch</blockquote>

<blockquote>"The German joke in our company: we have more pilots than Lufthansa. The problem is you get lost in pilot land. Instead of having one plan you really define, you're doing as much as you can. That's the first mistake." — Cedric Neike</blockquote>

<blockquote>"If the human doesn't trust the AI agent, it's not going to work. Basically, you are spending money for nothing. That's really what we have to do as a base: build trust." — Aiman Ezzat</blockquote>

<blockquote>"We have to get into this crisis mode. Crisis means you understand you have a problem. Number two is you focus on what to do and what not, and number three is speed, speed, speed." — Roland Busch</blockquote>

Please welcome Roland Busch to the stage. I want you to think back to the time before electricity. The world moved at the pace of people, horses bridged our distances, steam powered our engines, and ideas moved only as fast as a letter or a human voice. Then, electricity arrived—a general-purpose technology that turned night into day. It amplified human capabilities and empowered progress that once felt out of reach. At Siemens, we helped build that world: electric grids, telecommunication, the trains that move people and goods, and machines that accelerated production. One and a half centuries later, another general-purpose technology has arrived. And this time, it is not about energy; it is about intelligence. It is about Artificial Intelligence. And Artificial Intelligence will be as transformative for this century as electricity was for the last one. It is revolutionizing how we design, build, and operate infrastructure, making entire systems, grids, cities, and economies more adaptive and more efficient. Siemens helped build the world in the light of electricity, and now we do it again in the age of intelligence. We are powering the Industrial AI revolution. And it's moving faster than any transformation before. For steam, it took 60 years. Electricity took 30, computers 15, and AI 7. And by the way, half of that time is already gone. When AI enters a physical system, it stops being a feature; it becomes a force—a force with direct real-world impact. But AI that runs a production line, manages a power grid, or steers a logistics network has to be reliable in the real world. It must be reliable and safe. So, hallucination is not acceptable in Industrial AI. What's the solution? It is not about going more slowly as we develop AI for the real world. It is more about having embedded AI technology from the beginning, right from the beginning when you first start using it. And that's what sets Industrial AI apart from the AI on your phones. In the next 40 minutes, we will show you the tools that are ready today to help you make progress with Industrial AI and how you can use them to create value at every step: design, build, and operate. Well, it all starts with an idea. Ideas become designs, and from designs you start building. But long before you build anything, you want to know: how does it actually work? Siemens software helps you find those answers really very early in the process. Our tools allow you to build digital twins. Digital twins are replicas of whatever you want to create. They are physics-based, photorealistic, in 3D, and real-time. So, no matter whether it is a machine, a factory, or an entire logistics network, you run the twin. You stress test it, you break it—of course, you break it virtually—and then you spend not a single Euro on really building it, not before you know how the real thing works. And only when it works in the digital world, then you start building it in the real world. Our customers take it one step further. With our digital twin composer, they connect digital twins of machines, of production lines, and of entire buildings, and they make them play together in one integrated simulation. Take Kion, a leading logistics company. Their forklifts and automated vehicles keep warehouses moving and supply chains open. With our digital twin composer, the company can now test virtually how different warehouse layouts affect throughput before moving a single shelf. It looks fun and looks easy, yes, but all these simulations are hard indeed—seriously hard. Where it becomes almost, almost impossible is aerodynamics. That means, for example, calculating the air flows around a moving object like a car. Exciting stuff, at least for me, but then I'm a physicist after all. So what you see here is a wind tunnel simulation of a BMW, and normally these calculations take days. Now, with accelerated computing from Nvidia, we drive them by a factor of 10, 100, or sometimes even 1000 times faster. And that matters in highly competitive industries like automotive. Or take passenger experiences. To avoid uncomfortable vibrations, carmakers needed a complete 3D simulation model. This required hundreds of thousands of tiny rectangles or triangles on a mesh. Building and simulating just one model took weeks, and to test another one, you had to start all over again. Now, today we announce at VivaTech a novel software simulation. Simcenter Simsolid eliminates this testing and meshing entirely. Instead, straight from the design, you go into the calculation in your physics, and it takes minutes. Every structural calculation an engineer needs—vibration, thermal, fatigue—is now 30 times faster. Speed also matters if you plan to disrupt a whole industry as a startup. And that's why today we are announcing at VivaTech our software collections for startups. We give up to a 95 percent discount for startups for our design, engineering, and simulation collections, making faster product design and simulation much more affordable. Our technology really makes the difference. Take the example of Latitude, a French startup, and they build rocket engines, 3D printed for small satellite launches. They switched from a patchwork of tools to our software suite. The result: six months speed on project delivery and 15 percent higher engineering efficiency. That's how you turn ideas into design at the speed of AI. The next step is to engineer the production. Let's look at the reality as of today. First, manufacturing engineers create automation code. For this, they work on our TIA Portal. It's an engineering tool and a software engineering platform where they find everything they need: automation components, controllers, motors, switches, sensors—thousands of them. In this environment, engineers configure all components. They document what they do, and they test and debug until their code works perfectly. This takes time, a lot of time, and it is repetitive work on top. But this is about to change. Less than two months ago, we launched a new, revolutionary AI product for industrial automation. We call it the Industrial Copilot. And it's not an AI chatbot that only comes up with suggestions. It is an Industrial AI agent that completes engineering tasks end-to-end. So, it operates inside our TIA Portal and it works autonomously. You define the outcome, and the engineering agent makes a plan, collects the documents, and gathers all the data. It writes executable code for your controllers, compiles the whole thing, and validates it over and over again until the code works and is compiled without an error, ready to implement. What is the feedback from our customers? We see 50 percent higher productivity, 2.5 times faster development, and 80 percent higher quality. And today at VivaTech, we are announcing two new capabilities of our industrial engineering agent. It now additionally understands parts of automation projects—very important ones. Now it is grounded in plans from electrical design tools and automatically integrates that information into our TIA Portal, ready to use for all the engineers. What used to be days of manual work now takes minutes. Secondly, the industrial engineering agent has learned to build exactly along the common norms of your company. The agent now works with your engineering framework—that's how they call it—with hundreds of pages and documents, thousands of rules, and comes up with the correct software structure which is compliant to your frameworks. And that fits perfectly, and again, in minutes. These capabilities move our agent upstream in the workflow. That's what Industrial AI looks like. It's not hype, there is no hallucination, it's real impact. So, we have designed, we have built, and now the crucial moment starts: we bridge into the real world. Production. In a real factory where thousands of decisions need to happen in parallel with real-world consequences, Industrial AI finds the hidden potential in your machines and processes. It reads faster than any operator could and learns with every single cycle. So let's take a look at this welding line at an Audi plant. It had been running in the real world for years. What you see here is robot arms welding car bodies with millimeter precision. Then comes the quality check. Multiple cameras capture the process. They do that for 2000 welding spots per minute. And this piece of hardware, which is our Industrial PC, is checking 2000 welding spots a minute. It has a trained AI module in the cloud which runs on the edge. It works on an Nvidia GPU—it's the brain which we are bringing from the cloud to the shop floor—inspecting, evaluating, and reporting. And now imagine this intelligence across an entire factory. In Erlangen, Germany, we produce the components that make electric motors run efficiently. And we are turning this factory into the world's first fully AI-driven production site. So far, 100 AI algorithms work on the shop floor, using digital twins and intelligent automation. What's the impact? We see a 40 percent faster time to market, 42 percent lower energy consumption, and a 69 percent productivity increase. Imagine these results in your production sites. And we don't stop here. Erlangen is our blueprint for the factory of the future. Here we use physics AI to automate tasks that have simply been out of reach by reasonable cost. For example, it looks simple, placing a plastic bag of screws into a package. But these bags are flexible, difficult to grip, and unpredictable in shape. So, no conventional automation could handle this at a reasonable cost and with the highest quality requirements on the shop floor. Physics AI can. We taught the system what to do if something isn't placed correctly; it detects and corrects itself. Every factory has hundreds of tasks like this—tasks that were never worth automating until now. With two lightweight robotic arms, minimal programming, and maximum flexibility, our colleagues have more time for the work where they really need their expertise. So the tools are here. You might wonder: why do many companies get stuck in pilot after pilot rather than scaling? Let's learn from companies that are doing that successfully. For this, I would like to invite my fellow board member, Cedric Neike, to the stage. Thank you so much, Roland. Bonjour, Paris. I will do a quick experiment to wake you up. But at the end of the day, what I was trying to demonstrate is that we all talk about AI, we all use AI, and we all try to push it forward, but we fail. We fail particularly in a lot of industrial environments. So the question is: why is that the case? There is something which I call the three traps of adoption of AI in an industrial environment. I call them the industrial AI syndromes. There are three of them. Let me start with the first one. The first one, I call it the "spray and pray" syndrome. This is where you try to shoot AI at everything. You define somebody who is called a Chief AI Officer, and he does a lot of pilots. The joke—and the Germans are not very good at jokes—but the joke in our German company is that we have more pilots than Lufthansa, right? "On a plus de pilotes qu'Air France" would be the French sort of aspect of it. The problem is you get lost in pilot land. So, instead of having one plan you really define, you're doing as much as you can. That is the first mistake. The second mistake is what I call the foundation syndrome, right? Once you have these islands—and it was the same when you went to Industry 4.0—you had digital islands. They were all not connected. You had little pilots, little trials, and none of them were connected in some ways. I call it the foundation syndrome. So the hard thing you have to do is build the foundation within your company to be able to scale. You need to do it in your CRM system, in your PLM system, and in your ERP system. We talk so much about this huge treasure of data which sits in European companies, but if this treasure is not structured, you cannot access it. So, the second thing you need to do is build a foundation. And we are brutal about it at Siemens. We basically call it the data fabric. We are forcing everybody to have the same structural capability so we can build our models on it. So what's the third trap? It will appeal to a lot of the French people here: I call it the lonesome cowboy syndrome. What is it? It's a bit like Lucky Luke—you are really sort of doing it on your own. You think you don't need any help, and you just push the AI project on your own. But the reality is you are often falling into the trap of not being able to scale it. So, the three main elements are how you go about it. I'm going to bring somebody out to help me discuss this, and I'm going to bring you two very clear examples. But the three traps are very, very simple: first, you basically are trying to shoot at everything; second, you don't have a foundation; and third, you do it on your own. In order to do this, I want you to help me give a huge round of applause for Aiman, the CEO of Capgemini Group, who will help me give you an overview on how we do it. Thank you, Aiman. Hello. It is good to have you here. Aiman, I shared my three principles: the spray and pray, the missing foundation, and why it is so hard to bring AI into the industry as a lonesome cowboy. Give me a quick overview, a quick intro from your side. Yes, absolutely. We run Capgemini, and we have a great partnership with Siemens for many years. I know Cedric well; he speaks French. I pretend to. No, he's actually very good. Really, when we look at deploying industrial AI, it's a lot more complex and a lot more ambitious than using a generative AI tool for productivity. I mean, we're talking about a different world. We are talking about rewiring completely our organizations and designing completely new business models around two key elements. One, we're talking about new agentic products and services, and of course, here we leverage accelerated R&D cycles, AI-enhanced software, and physical products to be able to do that. And the second thing is the new agentic business processes. We are redesigning how work happens, blending employees and humans with AI agents to get the work done. But for that, as you all know, we need strong foundations. The first one is on the technology side, with solid data fundamentals. If you don't have that, it's very difficult to progress. Then, you deploy AI agents. AI agents are digital workers. For that, you need very strong governance, very good control mechanisms, and a solid operating model. If not, it's like deploying hundreds of employees across the company out of control, doing what they want. And you'd have chaos in your company if you don't have the proper governance to manage these agents. We talk about the control plane, about how we're going to manage all these AI agents. And beyond chaos, you'll also have a big consumption of tokens, so the governance of how we manage that is very important. And finally, you have the human side. Having AI agents and humans working side by side is not the simplest thing. Because if humans do not trust the AI agent, it's not going to work. Basically, you are spending money for nothing. So that's really what we have to do as a base to be able to succeed. Absolutely, and you talk about trust—humans and AI agents trusting each other. I think because it's a great complementarity, you know. On our side, we bring all the strategy and transformation capabilities, the end-to-end deployment in complex environments, and a lot of capability in terms of AI ecosystem management and, of course, new business models. We deploy that together across industries, from aerospace to consumer products, automotive, and water. And of course, we are building on the trust we have between each other and the trust we have with our clients. So one of those clients is GravitHy. Can you give us an overview of what you do with them? So, we are in Paris, right? You have the Eiffel Tower, which is 7300 tons of steel. Steel is a key component in our world today: bridges, cars, electric cars, and many other things. There is a small issue with steel, however: 8 percent of global CO2 emissions come from steel production. So GravitHy came up with the idea of building a green steel plant in Marseille. That is a 2.2 billion Euro investment. It's a small thing: we replace coal with hydrogen, which explains the "H-Y" at the end of the name. The reality of what we are aiming for is 2 million tons of steel produced every year, which means the equivalent of almost one Eiffel Tower every day. That is impressive. The only issue is that there is no pilot plant. You get the go-ahead only for a massive 2.2 billion investment, and if you miss it, you cannot go back. Hence, we have worked together to build a digital twin of the factory and of the entire operating model to be able to simulate all operations and make sure that we get it right. But also, we built an agentic AI layer on top of that so, from an operations perspective, we have a learning environment—pretty much a learning factory. It's really the control brain of the factory that learns how to optimize hydrogen consumption in real-time, do predictive maintenance, and so on. So not only do we have a great, optimized factory built, but it's also what we can call a learning factory that can basically learn and improve every day. And that's the advantage as we have talked about digital twins for the last 10 or 20 years. When you build a digital twin and supercharge it with AI to come up with the right solution very quickly, it makes a huge difference. Right? But let's stay in France, and let's look at another major player like Sanofi. So Sanofi, you know, GravitHy is about reinventing an industry. And of course, every industry has its own problems and its own challenges. In pharma, the mission sounds simple: you need to find the best, safest, and most effective medicine for patients. But getting there is quite complex. The challenge in pharma is that you have plenty of different factories and sites across the world, and it's a highly regulated industry. Much of what you're trying to do is figure out how to scale, which is a very challenging point. In regulated environments, people still manage a lot of data in a manual way, which makes it much more challenging to replicate from one environment to another. The issue is going to be scalability. How do you scale? At a level where you have 100 production lines across 50 manufacturing sites, scaling is not simple. So Sanofi has implemented what's called smart operations, a manufacturing execution system we helped develop with them. This is the cornerstone and the foundation of how you make it happen. It shows what needs to happen, records what actually happens, guides everybody step-by-step, and holds electronic batch records instead of manual paper. Instead of searching through documents, the team can see the exact information they need right in the right place. We are working with Sanofi and Siemens to roll that out across all manufacturing sites worldwide. So why is that so important? Everybody here knows Moore's Law, right? Moore's Law basically means you have an exponential advantage in terms of cost structure through the development of microchips, which we will share with you later. In pharma, we have something called Eroom's Law. If you spell Moore's Law the other way around, you get Eroom. Under this, every nine months the cost of developing a new drug doubles. It becomes more and more expensive to build drugs. So, we need to be able to use AI, and we need to be able to use digital twins. It takes 15 years to launch a new drug, plus another 3 years to put it into production. Building this all together actually makes a huge, huge difference. The impact is very concrete: we have a 70 percent advantage in batch reductions and 80 percent fewer deviations. This basically means that AI brings real value to the bottom line, which connects back to the question I asked all of you earlier. We talked about two industries and two companies. What did they do right? They did not launch a thousand AI projects. They built the foundation—the foundation to build and simulate digital twins, or the foundation to run their factories and their R&D. And they went with partners. They went with us, and they could also go with other partners. The takeaway for us is that it will become easier if you focus, if you build the foundation, and if you partner as much as possible. Aiman, thank you so much for joining us today and giving us this overview. Thank you. We talked about Eroom's Law, which meant we need to bring AI into the pharma world. Solar panels on the roof of a data center generate power, and guess what—it comes in DC. Batteries to store energy there use DC. Every chip runs on DC. When the entire system speaks the same language, obviously you do not want to waste power in conversions from AC, alternating current, to DC. That's why the whole AI factory will run on DC end-to-end. But when something goes wrong, direct current is much harder to switch. Here comes our circuit breaker. This is a new device that interrupts faults in milliseconds, which is 1000 times faster than conventional mechanical systems. Think about what you're protecting here: tens of thousands of high-performance GPUs worth millions. What If AI Doesn’t Ruin Everything? Understanding Artificial Intelligence With Reid Hoffman

This is Gavin Newsom(256.0K)·110:48·8.3K views·2026-06-19
Gavin NewsomGovernor of California Reid HoffmanCo-founder, LinkedIn
TL;DRWhile public anxiety over AI-driven job loss and concentrated wealth intensifies, tech pioneer Reid Hoffman argues that AI is an economic accelerant that must be steered rather than paused.
He advocates for a "humanist" approach to deploy AI to democratize medicine, law,...

TL;DR

While public anxiety over AI-driven job loss and concentrated wealth intensifies, tech pioneer Reid Hoffman argues that AI is an economic accelerant that must be steered rather than paused. He advocates for a "humanist" approach to deploy AI to democratize medicine, law, and education while warning against growing political corruption and anti-business policies.

Debunking the AI "Bloodbath" and Navigating Job Transitions

  • Macroeconomic Realities vs. AI Hype:
      • The 5% Factor: Hoffman asserts that current entry-level job freezes and tech layoffs are primarily driven by pandemic-era overhiring, remote-work restructurings, global tariff volatility, and macroeconomic caution—not AI. He estimates AI's actual contribution to current college-grad unemployment is only around 5%.
      • Executive Rationalization: CEOs frequently use AI as a scapegoat for layoffs to signal forward-thinking management to shareholders, avoiding blame for pandemic-era overhiring and mismanagement.
  • Reframing the "White-Collar Bloodbath":
      • Dario Amodei's Predictions: Addressing Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei’s prediction that AI could eliminate 50% of white-collar jobs by 2030, Hoffman characterizes "bloodbath" rhetoric as misleading. He advised Amodei to frame this transition as a massive, rapid "job transition" rather than outright destruction, to avoid sparking panic and demands for a development freeze.
  • The "Co-Pilot" Future:
      • Human-Agent Collaboration: Future workers will manage "teams of AI agents" to execute workflows rather than working in isolation. Hoffman emphasizes that humans will not be replaced by AI; they will be replaced by other humans who know how to use AI.
      • Opportunity in Crisis: College graduates should actively learn to "AI-ify" businesses rather than attempting to opt out of the technological shift.

AI Regulation, Acceleration, and "American Intelligence"

  • The Fallacy of the Precautionary Pause:
      • The Compliance Gap: Hoffman vehemently opposes a global pause on AI development. He argues that while ethical, humanist developers would comply with a pause, malicious actors and foreign adversaries (like China) would not. This would cede the technological high ground to actors with no interest in human rights or safety.
      • American Intelligence: Hoffman champions "accelerationism with a steer" to ensure that the global standard for AI is built on American values, which can then be shaped and regulated globally.
  • Building Trust via Transparency:
      • Moving Beyond "Just Trust Us": In a historically low-trust environment, tech companies must actively prove their trustworthiness. Hoffman highlights Anthropic's model of training AI using a transparent "constitution" (a set of ethical training principles) as a key blueprint for building public confidence.
      • California's SB 53: Hoffman praises California's frontier model regulatory framework for focusing on data, monitoring, and transparency rather than attempting to enforce broad, retroactive bans on development.

Democratizing AI and Solving the Compute-Energy Bottleneck

  • Sovereign Public Assistants: Hoffman proposes that state governments negotiate with hyperscalers (such as Microsoft, Google, OpenAI, and Anthropic) to provide three free, high-quality, 24/7 AI assistants to every citizen:
      • Medical Assistants: Providing democratized access to high-level health diagnostics.
      • Legal Assistants: Helping low-income individuals navigate contracts, tenant rights, and employment agreements without expensive attorney fees.
      • Educational Assistants: Providing tailored, personalized learning paths for students.
  • Negotiating Data Center Infrastructure: As data centers threaten to drive up residential energy costs (already representing 25% of Virginia's base load), governments must become better negotiators:
      • Power Offsets: Require data center developers to provision 130% power, contributing the excess 30% back to the local grid to lower residential utility bills.
      • Economic Reciprocity: Require tech hyperscalers to open physical offices and bring permanent corporate jobs to the local communities hosting their physical servers.
  • AI in Biology (Manus AI): Co-founded by Hoffman, Siddhartha Mukherjee, and Ujwal Singh, Manus AI acts as a drug discovery factory. By using scale compute to solve complex biological search problems, the company aims to discover novel, non-toxic small molecules and proteins to cure various cancers.

The Tech Elite, xAI, and the Rise of "Kleptocracy"

  • Elon Musk’s Transformation: Hoffman criticizes his former PayPal colleague as a "big narcissist" driven by a Messiah complex. He labels Musk’s lawsuit against OpenAI as "pathetic" and heavily critiques xAI for lacking safety guardrails in a desperate race for market share.
  • Corporate Corruption & Government Contracts: Hoffman notes the contradiction of Elon Musk publicly calling xAI's infrastructure a "train wreck" while simultaneously securing lucrative national security and government contracts.
  • The "Greatest Kleptocracy": Hoffman warns that the intersection of political power, crypto lobbying (such as World Liberty Financial and UFC crypto payments), and tech oligarchs has birthed "the greatest kleptocracy of our lifetimes," where policy and high-value silicon chips are traded for financial and political favors.

Reforming Democratic Economic Strategy and Taxation

  • A Pro-Business Democratic Shift: Hoffman urges Democrats to abandon anti-business rhetoric, asserting that "you cannot be pro-job and anti-business." Business revenue pays for the social safety net, Medicare, and Social Security.
  • Wealth Tax Pitfalls vs. Wealth Funds:
      • Catastrophic Capital Flight: While supporting progressive taxation, Hoffman warns that blunt-force, state-level billionaire taxes trigger massive capital flight. He personally knows 15 high-net-worth individuals who left California to avoid the wealth tax.
      • Sovereign Wealth Alternatives: Instead of punitive taxation, Hoffman advocates for a federal-level restructuring of capital gains and inheritance taxes, or pitching progressive taxes to the wealthy as contributions to a state sovereign wealth fund.
  • Resisting Political Intimidation: Hoffman details his choice to fund E. Jean Carroll’s civil lawsuit against Donald Trump, despite subsequent threats of retaliatory DOJ investigations. He describes receiving corrupt, backchannel propositions to make his "Trump problems go away" in exchange for $20 million donations, reinforcing his commitment to "not bend the knee" to authoritarian pressure.

Notable Quotes

"The humans aren’t replaced in all cases by AI. They’re replaced by humans using AI." — *Reid Hoffman*
"Elon became a narcissist... He lies through his teeth constantly... xAI is fine if we create a whole bunch of like pornographic images some of which are children, because it’s more important that I have this AI thing than the damage we're doing." — *Reid Hoffman*
"Someone gets you a billion dollars in crypto? Yeah, yes, you're bought. Period. We're living in the greatest kleptocracy of your and my lifetimes." — *Reid Hoffman*
"You can't be pro-job and anti-business... Stop doing anti-business rhetoric because people go, 'Wait, that's totally broken.'" — *Reid Hoffman*
